<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court therefore struck a balance, turning on the stage of the pregnancy at which the abortion would occur.  The Court explained that early on, a woman's choice must prevail, but that "at some point the state interests" become "dominant."  <em>Id.</em>, at 155.  It then set some guideposts.  In the first trimester of pregnancy, the State could not interfere at all with the decision to terminate a pregnancy.  At any time after that point, the State could regulate to protect the pregnant woman's health, such as by insisting that abortion providers and facilities meet safety requirements.  And after the fetus's viability--the point when the fetus "has the capability of meaningful life outside the mother's womb"--the State could ban abortions, except when necessary to preserve the woman's life or health.  <em>Id.</em>,<em> </em>at 163-164.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the 20 years between <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>, the Court expressly reaffirmed <em>Roe </em>on two occasions, and applied it on many more.  Recognizing that "arguments [against <em>Roe</em>] continue to be made," we responded that the doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em> "demands respect in a society governed by the rule of law."  <em>Akron</em> v. <em>Akron Center for Reproductive Health, Inc.</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 416, 419-420 (1983).  And we avowed that the "vitality" of "constitutional principles cannot be allowed to yield simply because of disagreement with them."  <em>Thornburgh</em> v. <em>American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists</em>, 476 U.&nbsp;S. 747, 759 (1986).  So the Court, over and over, enforced the constitutional principles <em>Roe </em>had declared.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Ohio</em> v. <em>Akron Center for Reproductive Health</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 502 (1990); <em>Hodgson</em> v. <em>Minnesota</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 417 (1990); <em>Simopoulos</em> v. <em>Virginia</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 506 (1983); <em>Planned Parenthood Assn. of Kansas City, Mo., Inc.</em> v. <em>Ashcroft</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 476 (1983); <em>H. L.</em> v. <em>Matheson</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S. 398 (1981); <em>Bellotti</em> v. <em>Baird</em>, 443 U.&nbsp;S. 622 (1979); <em>Planned Parenthood of Central Mo.</em> v. <em>Danforth</em>, 428 U.&nbsp;S. 52 (1976).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Then, in <em>Casey</em>, the Court considered the matter anew, and again upheld <em>Roe</em>'s core precepts.  <em>Casey </em>is in significant measure a precedent about the doctrine of precedent--until today, one of the Court's most important.  But we leave for later that aspect of the Court's decision.  The key thing now is the substantive aspect of the Court's considered conclusion that "the essential holding of <em>Roe</em> v. <em>Wade</em> should be retained and once again reaffirmed."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 846.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Central to that conclusion was a full-throated restatement of a woman's right to choose.  Like <em>Roe</em>, <em>Casey </em>grounded that right in the Fourteenth Amendment's guarantee of "liberty."  That guarantee encompasses realms of conduct not specifically referenced in the Constitution: "Marriage is mentioned nowhere" in that document, yet the Court was "no doubt correct" to protect the freedom to marry "against state interference."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 847-848.  And the guarantee of liberty encompasses conduct today that was not protected at the time of the Fourteenth Amendment.  See <em>id.</em>, at 848.  "It is settled now," the Court said--though it was not always so--that "the Constitution places limits on a State's right to interfere with a person's most basic decisions about family and parenthood, as well as bodily integrity."  <em>Id.</em>, at 849 (citations omitted); see <em>id.</em>, at 851 (similarly describing the constitutional protection given to "personal decisions relating to marriage, procreation, contraception, [and] family relationships").<em>  </em>Especially important in this web of precedents protecting an individual's most "personal choices" were those guaranteeing the right to contraception.  <em>Ibid.</em>; see <em>id.</em>, at 852-853.  In those cases, the Court had recognized "the right of the individual" to make the vastly consequential "decision whether to bear" a child.  <em>Id.</em>, at 851 (emphasis deleted).  So too, <em>Casey </em>reasoned, the liberty clause protects the decision of a woman confronting an unplanned pregnancy.  Her decision about abortion was central, in the same way, to her capacity to chart her life's course.  See <em>id.</em>, at 853.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In reaffirming the right <em>Roe </em>recognized, the Court took full account of the diversity of views on abortion, and the importance of various competing state interests.  Some Americans, the Court stated, "deem [abortion] nothing short of an act of violence against innocent human life."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 852.  And each State has an interest in "the protection of potential life"--as <em>Roe </em>itself had recognized.  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 871 (plurality opinion).  On the one hand, that interest was not conclusive.  The State could not "resolve" the "moral and spiritual" questions raised by abortion in "such a definitive way that a woman lacks all choice in the matter."  <em>Id.</em>, at 850 (majority opinion).  It could not force her to bear the "pain" and "physical constraints" of "carr[ying] a child to full term" when she would have chosen an early abortion.  <em>Id.</em>, at 852.  But on the other hand, the State had, as <em>Roe </em>had held, an exceptionally significant interest in disallowing abortions in the later phase of a pregnancy.  And it had an ever-present interest in "ensur[ing] that the woman's choice is informed" and in presenting the case for "choos[ing] childbirth over abortion."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 878 (plurality opinion).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;So <em>Casey </em>again struck a balance, differing from <em>Roe</em>'s in only incremental ways.  It retained <em>Roe</em>'s "central holding" that the State could bar abortion only after viability.  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 860 (majority opinion).  The viability line, <em>Casey</em> thought, was "more workable" than any other in marking the place where the woman's liberty interest gave way to a State's efforts to preserve potential life.  <em>Id.</em>, at 870 (plurality opinion).  At that point, a "second life" was capable of "independent existence."  <em>Ibid.  </em>If the woman even by then had not acted, she lacked adequate grounds to object to "the State's intervention on [the developing child's] behalf."  <em>Ibid.  </em>At the same time, <em>Casey </em>decided, based on two decades of experience, that the <em>Roe </em>framework did not give States sufficient ability to regulate abortion prior to viability.  In that period, <em>Casey </em>now made clear, the State could regulate not only to protect the woman's health but also to "promot[e] prenatal life."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 873 (plurality opinion).  In particular, the State could ensure informed choice and could try to promote childbirth.  See <em>id.</em>, at 877-878.  But the State still could not place an "undue burden"--or "substantial obstacle"--"in the path of a woman seeking an abortion."  <em>Id.</em>, at 878.  Prior to viability, the woman, consistent with the constitutional "meaning of liberty," must "retain the ultimate control over her destiny and her body."  <em>Id.</em>, at 869.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We make one initial point about this analysis in light of the majority's insistence that <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>, and we in defending them, are dismissive of a "State's interest in protecting prenatal life."  <em>Ante</em>, at 38.  Nothing could get those decisions more wrong.  As just described, <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em> invoked powerful state interests in that protection, operative at every stage of the pregnancy and overriding the woman's liberty after viability.  The strength of those state interests is exactly why the Court allowed greater restrictions on the abortion right than on other rights deriving from the Fourteenth Amendment.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 1" name="FRopinion1.1" href="#FNopinion1.1">1</a></sup>  But what <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>also recognized--which today's majority does not--is that a woman's freedom and equality are likewise involved.  That fact--the presence of countervailing interests--is what made the abortion question hard, and what necessitated balancing.  The majority scoffs at that idea, castigating us for "repeatedly prais[ing] the 'balance'&nbsp;" the two cases arrived at (with the word "balance" in scare quotes).  <em>Ante</em>, at 38.<em>  </em>To the majority "balance" is a dirty word, as moderation is a foreign concept.  The majority would allow States to ban abortion from conception onward because it does not think forced childbirth at all implicates a woman's rights to equality and freedom.  Today's Court, that is, does not think there is anything of constitutional significance attached to a woman's control of her body and the path of her life.  <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>thought that one-sided view misguided.  In some sense, that is the difference in a nutshell between our precedents and the majority opinion.  The constitutional regime we have lived in for the last 50 years recognized competing interests, and sought a balance between them.  The constitutional regime we enter today erases the woman's interest and recognizes only the State's (or the Federal Government's).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority makes this change based on a single question: Did the reproductive right recognized in <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>exist in "1868, the year when the Fourteenth Amendment was ratified"?  <em>Ante</em>, at 23.  The majority says (and with this much we agree) that the answer to this question is no: In 1868, there was no nationwide right to end a pregnancy, and no thought that the Fourteenth Amendment provided one.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Of course, the majority opinion refers as well to some later and earlier history.  On the one side of 1868, it goes back as far as the 13th (the 13th!) century.  See <em>ante</em>,<em> </em>at 17.  But that turns out to be wheel-spinning.  First, it is not clear what relevance such early history should have, even to the majority.  See <em>New York State Rifle &amp; Pistol Assn., Inc. </em>v. <em>Bruen</em>, 597 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2022) (slip op., at 26) ("Historical evidence that long predates [ratification] may not illuminate the scope of the right").  If the early history obviously supported abortion rights, the majority would no doubt say that only the views of the Fourteenth Amendment's ratifiers are germane.  See <em>ibid. </em>(It is "better not to go too far back into antiquity," except if olden "law survived to become our Founders' law").  Second--and embarrassingly for the majority--early law in fact does provide some support for abortion rights.  Common-law authorities did not treat abortion as a crime before "quickening"--the point when the fetus moved in the womb.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 2" name="FRopinion1.2" href="#FNopinion1.2">2</a></sup>  And early American law followed the common-law rule.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 3" name="FRopinion1.3" href="#FNopinion1.3">3</a></sup>  So the criminal law of that early time might be taken as roughly consonant with <em>Roe</em>'s and <em>Casey</em>'s different treatment of early and late abortions.  Better, then, to move forward in time.  On the other side of 1868, the majority occasionally notes that many States barred abortion up to the time of <em>Roe</em>.  See<em> ante</em>, at 24, 36.  That is convenient for the majority, but it is window dressing.  As the same majority (plus one) just informed us, "post-ratification adoption or acceptance of laws that are <em>inconsistent </em>with the original meaning of the constitutional text obviously cannot overcome or alter that text."  <em>New York State Rifle &amp; Pistol Assn., Inc.</em>, 597 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 27-28).  Had the pre-<em>Roe </em>liberalization of abortion laws occurred more quickly and more widely in the 20th century, the majority would say (once again) that only the ratifiers' views are germane.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority's core legal postulate, then, is that we in the 21st century must read the Fourteenth Amendment just as its ratifiers did.  And that is indeed what the majority emphasizes over and over again.  See <em>ante</em>, at 47 ("[T]he most important historical fact [is] how the States regulated abortion when the Fourteenth Amendment was adopted"); see also <em>ante</em>, at<em> </em>5, 16, and n. 24, 23, 25, 28.  If the ratifiers did not understand something as central to freedom, then neither can we.  Or said more particularly: If those people did not understand reproductive rights as part of the guarantee of liberty conferred in the Fourteenth Amendment, then those rights do not exist.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As an initial matter, note a mistake in the just preceding sentence.  We referred there to the "people" who ratified the Fourteenth Amendment: What rights did those "people" have in their heads at the time?  But, of course, "people" did not ratify the Fourteenth Amendment.  Men did.  So it is perhaps not so surprising that the ratifiers were not perfectly attuned to the importance of reproductive rights for women's liberty, or for their capacity to participate as equal members of our Nation.  Indeed, the ratifiers--both in 1868 and when the original Constitution was approved in 1788--did not understand women as full members of the community embraced by the phrase "We the People."  In 1868, the first wave of American feminists were explicitly told--of course by men--that it was not their time to seek constitutional protections.  (Women would not get even the vote for another half-century.)  To be sure, most women in 1868 also had a foreshortened view of their rights: If most men could not then imagine giving women control over their bodies, most women could not imagine having that kind of autonomy.  But that takes away nothing from the core point.  Those responsible for the original Constitution, including the Fourteenth Amendment, did not perceive women as equals, and did not recognize women's rights.  When the majority says that we must read our foundational charter as viewed at the time of ratification (except that we may also check it against the Dark Ages), it consigns women to second-class citizenship.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Casey </em>itself understood this point, as will become clear.  See <em>infra</em>, at 23-24.  It recollected with dismay a decision this Court issued just five years after the Fourteenth Amendment's ratification, approving a State's decision to deny a law license to a woman and suggesting as well that a woman had no legal status apart from her husband.  See 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 896-897 (majority opinion) (citing <em>Bradwell</em> v. <em>State</em>, 16 Wall. 130 (1873)).  "There was a time," <em>Casey </em>explained, when the Constitution did not protect "men and women alike."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 896.  But times had changed.  A woman's place in society had changed, and constitutional law had changed along with it.  The relegation of women to inferior status in either the public sphere or the family was "no longer consistent with our understanding" of the Constitution.  <em>Id.</em>, at 897.  Now, "[t]he Constitution protects all individuals, male or female," from "the abuse of governmental power" or "unjustified state interference."  <em>Id.</em>, at 896, 898.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;So how is it that, as <em>Casey </em>said, our Constitution, read now, grants rights to women, though it did not in 1868?  How is it that our Constitution subjects discrimination against them to heightened judicial scrutiny?  How is it that our Constitution, through the Fourteenth Amendment's liberty clause, guarantees access to contraception (also not legally protected in 1868) so that women can decide for themselves whether and when to bear a child?  How is it that until today, that same constitutional clause protected a woman's right, in the event contraception failed, to end a pregnancy in its earlier stages?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The answer is that this Court has rejected the majority's pinched view of how to read our Constitution.  "The Founders," we recently wrote, "knew they were writing a document designed to apply to ever-changing circumstances over centuries."  <em>NLRB </em>v. <em>Noel Canning</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S. 513, 533-534 (2014).  Or in the words of the great Chief Justice John Marshall, our Constitution is "intended to endure for ages to come," and must adapt itself to a future "seen dimly," if at all.  <em>McCulloch</em> v. <em>Maryland</em>, 4 Wheat. 316, 415 (1819).  That is indeed why our Constitution is written as it is.  The Framers (both in 1788 and 1868) understood that the world changes.  So they did not define rights by reference to the specific practices existing at the time.  Instead, the Framers defined rights in general terms, to permit future evolution in their scope and meaning.  And over the course of our history, this Court has taken up the Framers' invitation.  It has kept true to the Framers' principles by applying them in new ways, responsive to new societal understandings and conditions.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nowhere has that approach been more prevalent than in construing the majestic but open-ended words of the Fourteenth Amendment--the guarantees of "liberty" and "equality" for all.  And nowhere has that approach produced prouder moments, for this country and the Court.  Consider an example <em>Obergefell</em> used a few years ago.  The Court there confronted a claim, based on <em>Washington</em> v. <em>Glucksberg</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 702 (1997), that the Fourteenth Amendment "must be defined in a most circumscribed manner, with central reference to specific historical practices"--exactly the view today's majority follows.  <em>Obergefell</em>,<em> </em>576 U.&nbsp;S., at 671.  And the Court specifically rejected that view.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 4" name="FRopinion1.4" href="#FNopinion1.4">4</a></sup>  In doing so, the Court reflected on what the proposed, historically circumscribed approach would have meant for interracial marriage.  See <em>ibid.  </em>The Fourteenth Amendment's ratifiers did not think it gave black and white people a right to marry each other.  To the contrary, contemporaneous practice deemed that act quite as unprotected as abortion.  Yet the Court in <em>Loving</em> v. <em>Virginia</em>, 388 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1967), read the Fourteenth Amendment to embrace the Lovings' union.  If, <em>Obergefell </em>explained, "rights were defined by who exercised them in the past, then received practices could serve as their own continued justification"--even when they conflict with "liberty" and "equality" as later and more broadly understood.  576 U.&nbsp;S., at 671.  The Constitution does not freeze for all time the original view of what those rights guarantee, or how they apply.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That does not mean anything goes.  The majority wishes people to think there are but two alternatives: (1) accept the original applications of the Fourteenth Amendment and no others, or (2) surrender to judges' "own ardent views," ungrounded in law, about the "liberty that Americans should enjoy."  <em>Ante</em>, at 14.  At least, that idea is what the majority <em>sometimes</em> tries to convey.  At other times, the majority (or, rather, most of it) tries to assure the public that it has no designs on rights (for example, to contraception) that arose only in the back half of the 20th century--in other words, that it is happy to pick and choose, in accord with individual preferences.  See <em>ante</em>, at 32, 66, 71-72; <em>ante</em>, at 10 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh, J., </small></span>concurring); but see <em>ante</em>, at 3 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas, J.</small></span>, concurring).  But that is a matter we discuss later.  See <em>infra</em>, at 24-29.  For now, our point is different: It is that applications of liberty and equality can evolve while remaining grounded in constitutional principles, constitutional history, and constitutional precedents.  The second Justice Harlan discussed how to strike the right balance when he explained why he would have invalidated a State's ban on contraceptive use.  Judges, he said, are not "free to roam where unguided speculation might take them."  <em>Poe</em> v. <em>Ullman</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 497, 542 (1961) (dissenting opinion).  Yet they also must recognize that the constitutional "tradition" of this country is not captured whole at a single moment.  <em>Ibid.  </em>Rather, its meaning gains content from the long sweep of our history and from successive judicial precedents--each looking to the last and each seeking to apply the Constitution's most fundamental commitments to new conditions.  That is why Americans, to go back to <em>Obergefell</em>'s example, have a right to marry across racial lines.  And it is why, to go back to Justice Harlan's case, Americans have a right to use contraceptives so they can choose for themselves whether to have children.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;All that is what <em>Casey </em>understood.  <em>Casey </em>explicitly rejected the present majority's method.  "[T]he specific practices of States at the time of the adoption of the Fourteenth Amendment," <em>Casey </em>stated, do not "mark[&nbsp;] the outer limits of the substantive sphere of liberty which the Fourteenth Amendment protects."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 848.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 5" name="FRopinion1.5" href="#FNopinion1.5">5</a></sup>  To hold otherwise--as the majority does today--"would be inconsistent with our law."  <em>Id.</em>, at 847.  Why?  Because the Court has "vindicated [the] principle" over and over that (no matter the sentiment in 1868) "there is a realm of personal liberty which the government may not enter"--especially relating to "bodily integrity" and "family life."  <em>Id.</em>,<em> </em>at 847, 849, 851.<em>  Casey</em> described in detail the Court's contraception cases.  See <em>id.</em>, at 848-849, 851-853.  It noted decisions protecting the right to marry, including to someone of another race.  See <em>id.</em>, at 847-848 ("[I]nterracial marriage was illegal in most States in the 19th century, but the Court was no doubt correct in finding it to be an aspect of liberty protected against state interference").  In reviewing decades and decades of constitutional law, <em>Casey</em> could draw but one conclusion: Whatever was true in 1868, "[i]t is settled now, as it was when the Court heard arguments in <em>Roe </em>v. <em>Wade</em>, that the Constitution places limits on a State's right to interfere with a person's most basic decisions about family and parenthood."  <em>Id.</em>, at 849.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And that conclusion still held good, until the Court's intervention here.  It was settled at the time of <em>Roe</em>, settled at the time of <em>Casey</em>, and settled yesterday that the Constitution places limits on a State's power to assert control over an individual's body and most personal decisionmaking.  A multitude of decisions supporting that principle led to <em>Roe</em>'s recognition and <em>Casey</em>'s reaffirmation of the right to choose; and <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>in turn supported additional protections for intimate and familial relations.  The majority has embarrassingly little to say about those precedents.  It (literally) rattles them off in a single paragraph; and it implies that they have nothing to do with each other, or with the right to terminate an early pregnancy.  See <em>ante</em>, at 31-32 (asserting that recognizing a relationship among them, as addressing aspects of personal autonomy, would ineluctably "license fundamental rights" to illegal "drug use [and] prostitution").  But that is flat wrong.  The Court's precedents about bodily autonomy, sexual and familial relations, and procreation are all interwoven--all part of the fabric of our constitutional law, and because that is so, of our lives.  Especially women's lives, where they safeguard a right to self-determination.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And eliminating that right, we need to say before further describing our precedents, is not taking a "neutral" position, as <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span> tries to argue.  <em>Ante</em>, at 2-3, 5, 7, 11-12 (concurring opinion).  His idea is that neutrality lies in giving the abortion issue to the States, where some can go one way and some another.  But would he say that the Court is being "scrupulously neutral" if it allowed New York and California to ban all the guns they want?  <em>Ante</em>, at 3.<em>  </em>If the Court allowed some States to use unanimous juries and others not?  If the Court told the States: Decide for yourselves whether to put restrictions on church attendance?  We could go on--and in fact we will.  Suppose <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh </small></span>were to say (in line with the majority opinion) that the rights we just listed are more textually or historically grounded than the right to choose.  What, then, of the right to contraception or same-sex marriage?  Would it be "scrupulously neutral" for the Court to eliminate those rights too?  The point of all these examples is that when it comes to rights, the Court does not act "neutrally" when it leaves everything up to the States.  Rather, the Court acts neutrally when it protects the right against all comers.  And to apply that point to the case here: When the Court decimates a right women have held for 50 years, the Court is not being "scrupulously neutral."  It is instead taking sides: against women who wish to exercise the right, and for States (like Mississippi) that want to bar them from doing so.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span> cannot obscure that point by appropriating the rhetoric of even-handedness.  His position just is what it is: A brook-no-compromise refusal to recognize a woman's right to choose, from the first day of a pregnancy.  And that position, as we will now show, cannot be squared with this Court's longstanding view that women indeed have rights (whatever the state of the world in 1868) to make the most personal and consequential decisions about their bodies and their lives.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider first, then, the line of this Court's cases protecting "bodily integrity."  <em>Casey</em>,<em> </em>505 U.&nbsp;S., at 849.  "No right," in this Court's time-honored view, "is held more sacred, or is more carefully guarded," than "the right of every individual to the possession and control of his own person."  <em>Union Pacific R.&nbsp;Co. </em>v.<em> Botsford</em>, 141 U.&nbsp;S. 250, 251 (1891); see <em>Cruzan</em> v. <em>Director, Mo. Dept. of Health</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 261, 269 (1990) (Every adult "has a right to determine what shall be done with his own body").  Or to put it more simply: Everyone, including women, owns their own bodies.  So the Court has restricted the power of government to interfere with a person's medical decisions or compel her to undergo medical procedures or treatments.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Winston</em> v. <em>Lee</em>, 470 U.&nbsp;S. 753, 766-767 (1985) (forced surgery); <em>Rochin </em>v. <em>California</em>, 342 U.&nbsp;S. 165, 166, 173-174 (1952) (forced stomach pumping); <em>Washington</em> v. <em>Harper</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 210, 229, 236 (1990) (forced administration of antipsychotic drugs).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Casey </em>recognized the "doctrinal affinity" between those precedents and <em>Roe</em>.  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 857.  And that doctrinal affinity is born of a factual likeness.  There are few greater incursions on a body than forcing a woman to complete a pregnancy and give birth.  For every woman, those experiences involve all manner of physical changes, medical treatments (including the possibility of a cesarean section), and medical risk.  Just as one example, an American woman is 14 times more likely to die by carrying a pregnancy to term than by having an abortion.  See <em>Whole Woman's Health</em> v. <em>Hellerstedt</em>, 579 U.&nbsp;S. 582, 618 (2016).  That women happily undergo those burdens and hazards of their own accord does not lessen how far a State impinges on a woman's body when it compels her to bring a pregnancy to term.  And for some women, as <em>Roe </em>recognized, abortions are medically necessary to prevent harm.  See 410 U.&nbsp;S., at 153.  The majority does not say--which is itself ominous--whether a State may prevent a woman from obtaining an abortion when she and her doctor have determined it is a needed medical treatment.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;So too, <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>fit neatly into a long line of decisions protecting from government intrusion a wealth of private choices about family matters, child rearing, intimate relationships, and procreation.  See <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 851, 857; <em>Roe</em>, 410 U.&nbsp;S., at 152-153;<em> </em>see also <em>ante</em>, at 31-32 (listing the myriad decisions of this kind that <em>Casey </em>relied on).  Those cases safeguard particular choices about whom to marry; whom to have sex with; what family members to live with; how to raise children--and crucially, whether and when to have children.  In varied cases, the Court explained that those choices--"the most intimate and personal" a person can make--reflect fundamental aspects of personal identity; they define the very "attributes of personhood."  <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 851.  And they inevitably shape the nature and future course of a person's life (and often the lives of those closest to her).  So, the Court held, those choices belong to the individual, and not the government.  That is the essence of what liberty requires.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And liberty may require it, this Court has repeatedly said, even when those living in 1868 would not have recognized the claim--because they would not have seen the person making it as a full-fledged member of the community.  Throughout our history, the sphere of protected liberty has expanded, bringing in individuals formerly excluded.  In that way, the constitutional values of liberty and equality go hand in hand; they do not inhabit the hermetically sealed containers the majority portrays.  Compare <em>Obergefell</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at 672-675, with <em>ante</em>, at 10-11.  So before <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>, the Court expanded in successive cases those who could claim the right to marry--though their relationships would have been outside the law's protection in the mid-19th century.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Loving</em>, 388 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (interracial couples); <em>Turner</em> v. <em>Safley</em>, 482 U.&nbsp;S. 78 (1987) (prisoners); see also, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Stanley</em> v. <em>Illinois</em>, 405 U.&nbsp;S. 645, 651-652 (1972) (offering constitutional protection to untraditional "family unit[s]").  And after <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>, of course, the Court continued in that vein.  With a critical stop to hold that the Fourteenth Amendment protected same-sex intimacy, the Court resolved that the Amendment also conferred on same-sex couples the right to marry.  See <em>Lawrence</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 558; <em>Obergefell</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S.<em> </em>644.  In considering that question, the Court held, "[h]istory and tradition," especially as reflected in the course of our precedent, "guide and discipline [the] inquiry."  <em>Id.</em>, at 664.  But the sentiments of 1868 alone do not and cannot "rule the present."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Casey </em>similarly recognized the need to extend the constitutional sphere of liberty to a previously excluded group.  The Court then understood, as the majority today does not, that the men who ratified the Fourteenth Amendment and wrote the state laws of the time did not view women as full and equal citizens.  See <em>supra</em>, at 15.  A woman then, <em>Casey </em>wrote, "had no legal existence separate from her husband."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 897.  Women were seen only "as the center of home and family life," without "full and independent legal status under the Constitution."  <em>Ibid.  </em>But that could not be true any longer: The State could not now insist on the historically dominant "vision of the woman's role."  <em>Id.</em>, at 852.  And equal citizenship, <em>Casey </em>realized, was inescapably connected to reproductive rights.  "The ability of women to participate equally" in the "life of the Nation"--in all its economic, social, political, and legal aspects--"has been facilitated by their ability to control their reproductive lives."  <em>Id.</em>, at 856.  Without the ability to decide whether and when to have children, women could not--in the way men took for granted--determine how they would live their lives, and how they would contribute to the society around them.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;For much that reason, <em>Casey </em>made clear that the precedents <em>Roe </em>most closely tracked were those involving contraception.  Over the course of three cases, the Court had held that a right to use and gain access to contraception was part of the Fourteenth Amendment's guarantee of liberty.  See <em>Griswold</em>, 381 U.&nbsp;S. 479; <em>Eisenstadt</em>, 405 U.&nbsp;S. 438; <em>Carey</em> v. <em>Population Services Int'l</em>, 431 U.&nbsp;S. 678 (1977).  That clause, we explained, necessarily conferred a right "to be free from unwarranted governmental intrusion into matters so fundamentally affecting a person as the decision whether to bear or beget a child."  <em>Eisenstadt</em>, 405 U.&nbsp;S., at 453; see <em>Carey</em>, 431 U.&nbsp;S., at 684-685.  <em>Casey </em>saw <em>Roe </em>as of a piece: In "critical respects the abortion decision is of the same character."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 852.  "[R]easonable people," the Court noted, could also oppose contraception; and indeed, they could believe that "some forms of contraception" similarly implicate a concern with "potential life."  <em>Id.</em>, at 853, 859.  Yet the views of others could not automatically prevail against a woman's right to control her own body and make her own choice about whether to bear, and probably to raise, a child.  When an unplanned pregnancy is involved--because either contraception or abortion is outlawed--"the liberty of the woman is at stake in a sense unique to the human condition."  <em>Id.</em>, at 852.  No State could undertake to resolve the moral questions raised "in such a definitive way" as to deprive a woman of all choice.  <em>Id.</em>, at 850.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Faced with all these connections between <em>Roe/Casey </em>and judicial decisions recognizing other constitutional rights, the majority tells everyone not to worry.  It can (so it says) neatly extract the right to choose from the constitutional edifice without affecting any associated rights.  (Think of someone telling you that the Jenga tower simply will not collapse.)  Today's decision, the majority first says, "does not undermine" the decisions cited by <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey--</em>the ones involving "marriage, procreation, contraception, [and] family relationships"--"in any way."  <em>Ante</em>, at 32; <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 851.  Note that this first assurance does not extend to rights recognized after <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>,<em> </em>and partly based on them--in particular, rights to same-sex intimacy and marriage.  See <em>supra</em>, at 23.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 6" name="FRopinion1.6" href="#FNopinion1.6">6</a></sup>  On its later tries, though, the majority includes those too: "Nothing in this opinion should be understood to cast doubt on precedents that do not concern abortion."  <em>Ante</em>, at 66; see <em>ante</em>, at 71-72.  That right is unique, the majority asserts, "because [abortion] terminates life or potential life."  <em>Ante</em>, at 66 (internal quotation marks omitted);<em> </em>see <em>ante</em>, at 32, 71-72.  So the majority depicts today's decision as "a restricted railroad ticket, good for this day and train only."  <em>Smith</em> v. <em>Allwright</em>, 321 U.&nbsp;S. 649, 669 (1944) (Roberts, J., dissenting).  Should the audience for these too-much-repeated protestations be duly satisfied?  We think not.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The first problem with the majority's account comes from <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span>'s concurrence--which makes clear he is not with the program.  In saying that nothing in today's opinion casts doubt on non-abortion precedents, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> explains, he means only that they are not at issue in this very case.  See <em>ante</em>, at 7 ("[T]his case does not present the opportunity to reject" those precedents).  But he lets us know what he wants to do when they are.  "[I]n future cases," he says, "we should reconsider all of this Court's substantive due process precedents, including <em>Griswold</em>, <em>Lawrence</em>, and <em>Obergefell</em>."  <em>Ante</em>, at 3; see also <em>supra</em>,<em> </em>at 25, and n.&nbsp;6.<em>  </em>And when we reconsider them?  Then "we have a duty" to "overrul[e] these demonstrably erroneous decisions."  <em>Ante</em>, at 3.<em>  </em>So at least one Justice is planning to use the ticket of today's decision again and again and again.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even placing the concurrence to the side, the assurance in today's opinion still does not work.  Or at least that is so if the majority is serious about its sole reason for overturning <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>: the legal status of abortion in the 19th century.  Except in the places quoted above, the state interest in protecting fetal life plays no part in the majority's analysis.  To the contrary, the majority takes pride in not expressing a view "about the status of the fetus."  <em>Ante</em>, at 65; see <em>ante</em>, at 32 (aligning itself with <em>Roe</em>'s<em> </em>and <em>Casey</em>'s stance of not deciding whether life or potential life is involved); <em>ante</em>, at 38-39 (similar).  The majority's departure from <em>Roe </em>and<em> Casey</em> rests instead--and only--on whether a woman's decision to end a pregnancy involves any Fourteenth Amendment liberty interest (against which <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>balanced the state interest in preserving fetal life).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 7" name="FRopinion1.7" href="#FNopinion1.7">7</a></sup>  According to the majority, no liberty interest is present--because (and only because) the law offered no protection to the woman's choice in the 19th century.  But here is the rub.  The law also did not then (and would not for ages) protect a wealth of other things.  It did not protect the rights recognized in <em>Lawrence </em>and <em>Obergefell </em>to same-sex intimacy and marriage.  It did not protect the right recognized in <em>Loving </em>to marry across racial lines.  It did not protect the right recognized in <em>Griswold </em>to contraceptive use.  For that matter, it did not protect the right recognized in <em>Skinner</em> v. <em>Oklahoma ex rel. Williamson</em>, 316 U.&nbsp;S. 535 (1942), not to be sterilized without consent.  So if the majority is right in its legal analysis, all those decisions were wrong, and all those matters properly belong to the States too--whatever the particular state interests involved.  And if that is true, it is impossible to understand (as a matter of logic and principle) how the majority can say that its opinion today does not threaten--does not even "undermine"--any number of other constitutional rights.  <em>Ante</em>, at 32.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 8" name="FRopinion1.8" href="#FNopinion1.8">8</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor does it even help just to take the majority at its word.  Assume the majority is sincere in saying, for whatever reason, that it will go so far and no further.  Scout's honor.  Still, the future significance of today's opinion will be decided in the future.  And law often has a way of evolving without regard to original intentions--a way of actually following where logic leads, rather than tolerating hard-toexplain lines.  Rights can expand in that way.  Dissenting in <em>Lawrence</em>, Justice Scalia explained why he took no comfort in the Court's statement that a decision recognizing the right to same-sex intimacy did "not involve" same-sex marriage.  539 U.&nbsp;S., at 604.  That could be true, he wrote, "only if one entertains the belief that principle and logic have nothing to do with the decisions of this Court."  <em>Id.</em>, at 605.<em>  </em>Score one for the dissent, as a matter of prophecy.  And logic and principle are not one-way ratchets.  Rights can contract in the same way and for the same reason--because whatever today's majority might say, one thing really does lead to another.  We fervently hope that does not happen because of today's decision.  We hope that we will not join Justice Scalia in the book of prophets.  But we cannot understand how anyone can be confident that today's opinion will be the last of its kind.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider, as our last word on this issue, contraception.  The Constitution, of course, does not mention that word.  And there is no historical right to contraception, of the kind the majority insists on.  To the contrary, the American legal landscape in the decades after the Civil War was littered with bans on the sale of contraceptive devices.  So again, there seem to be two choices.  See <em>supra</em>, at 5, 26-27.  If the majority is serious about its historical approach, then <em>Griswold </em>and its progeny are in the line of fire too.  Or if it is not serious, then .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. what <em>is </em>the basis of today's decision?  If we had to guess, we suspect the prospects of this Court approving bans on contraception are low.  But once again, the future significance of today's opinion will be decided in the future.  At the least, today's opinion will fuel the fight to get contraception, and any other issues with a moral dimension, out of the Fourteenth Amendment and into state legislatures.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 9" name="FRopinion1.9" href="#FNopinion1.9">9</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Anyway, today's decision, taken on its own, is catastrophic enough.  As a matter of constitutional method, the majority's commitment to replicate in 2022 every view about the meaning of liberty held in 1868 has precious little to recommend it.  Our law in this constitutional sphere, as in most, has for decades upon decades proceeded differently.  It has considered fundamental constitutional principles, the whole course of the Nation's history and traditions, and the step-by-step evolution of the Court's precedents.  It is disciplined but not static.  It relies on accumulated judgments, not just the sentiments of one long-ago generation of men (who themselves believed, and drafted the Constitution to reflect, that the world progresses).  And by doing so, it includes those excluded from that olden conversation, rather than perpetuating its bounds.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As a matter of constitutional substance, the majority's opinion has all the flaws its method would suggest.  Because laws in 1868 deprived women of any control over their bodies, the majority approves States doing so today.  Because those laws prevented women from charting the course of their own lives, the majority says States can do the same again.  Because in 1868, the government could tell a pregnant woman--even in the first days of her pregnancy--that she could do nothing but bear a child, it can once more impose that command.  Today's decision strips women of agency over what even the majority agrees is a contested and contestable moral issue.  It forces her to carry out the State's will, whatever the circumstances and whatever the harm it will wreak on her and her family.  In the Fourteenth Amendment's terms, it takes away her liberty.  Even before we get to <em>stare decisis</em>, we dissent.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">II</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;By overruling <em>Roe</em>, <em>Casey</em>, and more than 20 cases reaffirming or applying the constitutional right to abortion, the majority abandons <em>stare decisis</em>, a principle central to the rule of law<em>. </em> "<em>Stare decisis</em>" means "to stand by things decided."  Black's Law Dictionary 1696 (11th ed. 2019).  Blackstone called it the "established rule to abide by former precedents."  1 Blackstone 69.  <em>Stare decisis </em>"promotes the evenhanded, predictable, and consistent development of legal principles."  <em>Payne</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S., at 827.  It maintains a stability that allows people to order their lives under the law.  See H. Hart &amp; A. Sacks, The Legal Process: Basic Problems in the Making and Application of Law 568-569 (1994).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Stare decisis </em>also "contributes to the integrity of our constitutional system of government" by ensuring that decisions "are founded in the law rather than in the proclivities of individuals."  <em>Vasquez</em>, 474 U.&nbsp;S., at 265.  As Hamilton wrote: It "avoid[s] an arbitrary discretion in the courts."  The Federalist No. 78, p. 529 (J. Cooke ed. 1961) (A. Hamilton).  And as Blackstone said before him: It "keep[s] the scale of justice even and steady, and not liable to waver with every new judge's opinion."  1 Blackstone 69.  The "glory" of our legal system is that it "gives preference to precedent rather than .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. jurists."  H. Humble, Departure From Precedent, 19 Mich. L.&nbsp;Rev. 608, 614 (1921).  That is why, the story goes, Chief Justice John Marshall donned a plain black robe when he swore the oath of office.  That act personified an American tradition.  Judges' personal preferences do not make law; rather, the law speaks through them.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That means the Court may not overrule a decision, even a constitutional one, without a "special justification."  <em>Gamble</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 587 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2019) (slip op., at 11).  <em>Stare decisis</em> is, of course, not an "inexorable command"; it is sometimes appropriate to overrule an earlier decision.  <em>Pearson</em> v. <em>Callahan</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 223, 233 (2009).  But the Court must have a good reason to do so over and above the belief "that the precedent was wrongly decided." <em>Halliburton Co.</em> v. <em>Erica P. John Fund, Inc.</em>, 573 U.&nbsp;S. 258, 266 (2014).  "[I]t is not alone sufficient that we would decide a case differently now than we did then."  <em>Kimble</em> v. <em>Marvel Entertainment, LLC</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 446, 455 (2015).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority today lists some 30 of our cases as overruling precedent, and argues that they support overruling <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  But none does, as further described below and in the Appendix.  See <em>infra</em>, at 61-66.  In some, the Court only partially modified or clarified a precedent.  And in the rest, the Court relied on one or more of the traditional <em>stare decisis</em> factors in reaching its conclusion.  The Court found, for example, (1) a change in legal doctrine that undermined or made obsolete the earlier decision; (2) a factual change that had the same effect; or (3) an absence of reliance because the earlier decision was less than a decade old.  (The majority is wrong when it says that we insist on a test of changed law or fact alone, although that is present in most of the cases.  See <em>ante</em>, at 69.)  None of those factors apply here: Nothing--and in particular, no significant legal or factual change--supports overturning a half-century of settled law giving women control over their reproductive lives.  &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;First, for all the reasons we have given, <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>were correct.  In holding that a State could not "resolve" the debate about abortion "in such a definitive way that a woman lacks all choice in the matter," the Court protected women's liberty and women's equality in a way comporting with our Fourteenth Amendment precedents.  <em>Casey</em>,<em> </em>505 U.&nbsp;S., at 850.  Contrary to the majority's view, the legal status of abortion in the 19th century does not weaken those decisions.  And the majority's repeated refrain about "usurp[ing]" state legislatures' "power to address" a publicly contested question does not help it on the key issue here.  <em>Ante</em>, at 44; see <em>ante</em>, at 1.  To repeat: The point of a right is to shield individual actions and decisions "from the vicissitudes of political controversy, to place them beyond the reach of majorities and officials and to establish them as legal principles to be applied by the courts."  <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S., at 638; <em>supra</em>, at 7.  However divisive, a right is not at the people's mercy.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In any event "[w]hether or not we .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. agree" with a prior precedent is the beginning, not the end, of our analysis--and the remaining "principles of <em>stare decisis </em>weigh heavily against overruling" <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  <em>Dickerson</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 428, 443 (2000).  <em>Casey </em>itself applied those principles, in one of this Court's most important precedents about precedent.  After assessing the traditional <em>stare decisis</em> factors, <em>Casey</em> reached the only conclusion possible--that <em>stare decisis</em> operates powerfully here.  It still does.  The standards <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>set out are perfectly workable.  No changes in either law or fact have eroded the two decisions.  And tens of millions of American women have relied, and continue to rely, on the right to choose.  So under traditional <em>stare decisis</em> principles, the majority has no special justification for the harm it causes.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And indeed, the majority comes close to conceding that point.  The majority barely mentions any legal or factual changes that have occurred since <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  It suggests that the two decisions are hard for courts to implement, but cannot prove its case.  In the end, the majority says, all it must say to override <em>stare decisis</em> is one thing: that it believes <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>"egregiously wrong."  <em>Ante</em>, at 70.  That rule could equally spell the end of any precedent with which a bare majority of the present Court disagrees.  So how does that approach prevent the "scale of justice" from "waver[ing] with every new judge's opinion"?  1 Blackstone 69.  It does not.  It makes radical change too easy and too fast, based on nothing more than the new views of new judges.  The majority has overruled <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>for one and only one reason: because it has always despised them, and now it has the votes to discard them.  The majority thereby substitutes a rule by judges for the rule of law.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Contrary to the majority's view, there is nothing unworkable about <em>Casey</em>'s "undue burden" standard.  Its primary focus on whether a State has placed a "substantial obstacle" on a woman seeking an abortion is "the sort of inquiry familiar to judges across a variety of contexts."  <em>June Medical Services L.</em>&nbsp;<em>L.</em>&nbsp;<em>C.</em> v. <em>Russo</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2020) (slip op., at 6) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts</small></span>, C.&nbsp;J., concurring in judgment).  And it has given rise to no more conflict in application than many standards this Court and others unhesitatingly apply every day.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;General standards, like the undue burden standard, are ubiquitous in the law, and particularly in constitutional adjudication.  When called on to give effect to the Constitution's broad principles, this Court often crafts flexible standards that can be applied case-by-case to a myriad of unforeseeable circumstances.  See <em>Dickerson</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S., at 441 ("No court laying down a general rule can possibly foresee the various circumstances" in which it must apply). <em> </em>So, for example, the Court asks about undue or substantial burdens on speech, on voting, and on interstate commerce.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Arizona Free Enterprise Club's Freedom Club PAC</em> v. <em>Bennett</em>, 564 U.&nbsp;S. 721, 748 (2011); <em>Burdick</em> v. <em>Takushi</em>, 504 U.&nbsp;S. 428, 433-434 (1992); <em>Pike</em> v. <em>Bruce Church, Inc.</em>, 397 U.&nbsp;S. 137, 142 (1970).<em>  </em>The <em>Casey </em>undue burden standard is the same.  It also resembles general standards that courts work with daily in other legal spheres--like the "rule of reason" in antitrust law or the "arbitrary and capricious" standard for agency decisionmaking.  See <em>Standard Oil Co. of N.&nbsp;J.</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 221 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 62 (1911); <em>Motor Vehicle Mfrs. Assn. of United States, Inc.</em> v. <em>State Farm Mut. Automobile Ins. Co.</em>, 463 U.&nbsp;S. 29, 42-43 (1983).  Applying general standards to particular cases is, in many contexts, just what it means to do law.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And the undue burden standard has given rise to no unusual difficulties.  Of course, it has provoked some disagreement among judges.  <em>Casey </em>knew it would: That much "is to be expected in the application of any legal standard which must accommodate life's complexity."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 878 (plurality opinion).  Which is to say: That much is to be expected in the application of any legal standard.  But the majority vastly overstates the divisions among judges applying the standard.  We count essentially two.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> disagreed with other Justices in the <em>June Medical </em>majority about whether <em>Casey </em>called for weighing the benefits of an abortion regulation against its burdens.  See 591 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (slip op., at 6-7); <em>ante</em>, at 59, 60, and n.&nbsp;53.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 10" name="FRopinion1.10" href="#FNopinion1.10">10</a></sup>  We agree that the <em>June Medical </em>difference is a difference--but not one that would actually make a difference in the result of most cases (it did not in <em>June Medical</em>), and not one incapable of resolution were it ever to matter.  As for lower courts, there is now a one-year-old, one-to-one Circuit split about how the undue burden standard applies to state laws that ban abortions for certain reasons, like fetal abnormality.  See <em>ante</em>, at 61, and n. 57.  That is about it, as far as we can see.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 11" name="FRopinion1.11" href="#FNopinion1.11">11</a></sup>  And that is not much.  This Court mostly does not even grant certiorari on one-year-old, one-to-one Circuit splits, because we know that a bit of disagreement is an inevitable part of our legal system.  To borrow an old saying that might apply here: Not one or even a couple of swallows can make the majority's summer.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Anyone concerned about workability should consider the majority's substitute standard.  The majority says a law regulating or banning abortion "must be sustained if there is a rational basis on which the legislature could have thought that it would serve legitimate state interests."  <em>Ante</em>, at 77.  And the majority lists interests like "respect for and preservation of prenatal life," "protection of maternal health," elimination of certain "medical procedures," "mitigation of fetal pain," and others.  <em>Ante</em>, at 78.  This Court will surely face critical questions about how that test applies.  Must a state law allow abortions when necessary to protect a woman's life and health?  And if so, exactly when?  How much risk to a woman's life can a State force her to incur, before the Fourteenth Amendment's protection of life kicks in?  Suppose a patient with pulmonary hypertension has a 30-to-50 percent risk of dying with ongoing pregnancy; is that enough?  And short of death, how much illness or injury can the State require her to accept, consistent with the Amendment's protection of liberty and equality?  Further, the Court may face questions about the application of abortion regulations to medical care most people view as quite different from abortion.  What about the morning-after pill?  IUDs?  In vitro fertilization?  And how about the use of dilation and evacuation or medication for miscarriage management?  See generally L. Harris, Navigating Loss of Abortion Services--A Large Academic Medical Center Prepares for the Overturn of <em>Roe </em>v. <em>Wade</em>, 386 New England J. Med. 2061 (2022).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 12" name="FRopinion1.12" href="#FNopinion1.12">12</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, the majority's ruling today invites a host of questions about interstate conflicts.  See <em>supra</em>, at 3; see generally D. Cohen, G. Donley, &amp; R. Rebouché, The New Abortion Battleground, 123 Colum. L.&nbsp;Rev. (forthcoming 2023), https://ssrn.com/abstract=4032931.  Can a State bar women from traveling to another State to obtain an abortion?  Can a State prohibit advertising out-of-state abortions or helping women get to out-of-state providers?  Can a State interfere with the mailing of drugs used for medication abortions?  The Constitution protects travel and speech and interstate commerce, so today's ruling will give rise to a host of new constitutional questions.  Far from removing the Court from the abortion issue, the majority puts the Court at the center of the coming "interjurisdictional abortion wars."  <em>Id.</em>, at ___<em> </em>(draft, at 1).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In short, the majority does not save judges from unwieldy tests or extricate them from the sphere of controversy.  To the contrary, it discards a known, workable, and predictable standard in favor of something novel and probably far more complicated.  It forces the Court to wade further into hotly contested issues, including moral and philosophical ones, that the majority criticizes <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>for addressing.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When overruling constitutional precedent, the Court has almost always pointed to major legal or factual changes undermining a decision's original basis.  A review of the Appendix to this dissent proves the point.  See <em>infra</em>, at 61-66.  Most "successful proponent[s] of overruling precedent," this Court once said, have carried "the heavy burden of persuading the Court that changes in society or in the law dictate that the values served by <em>stare decisis</em> yield in favor of a greater objective."  <em>Vasquez</em>, 474 U.&nbsp;S., at 266.  Certainly, that was so of the main examples the majority cites: <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Board of Education</em>, 347 U.&nbsp;S. 483 (1954), and <em>West Coast Hotel Co.</em> v. <em>Parrish</em>, 300 U.&nbsp;S. 379 (1937).  But it is not so today.  Although nodding to some arguments others have made about "modern developments," the majority does not really rely on them, no doubt seeing their slimness.  <em>Ante</em>, at 33; see <em>ante</em>, at 34.  The majority briefly invokes the current controversy over abortion.  See <em>ante</em>, at 70-71.  But it has to acknowledge that the same dispute has existed for decades: Conflict over abortion is not a change but a constant.  (And as we will later discuss, the presence of that continuing division provides more of a reason to stick with, than to jettison, existing precedent.  See <em>infra</em>, at 55-57.)  In the end, the majority throws longstanding precedent to the winds without showing that anything significant has changed to justify its radical reshaping of the law.  See <em>ante</em>, at 43.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Subsequent legal developments have only reinforced <em>Roe</em> and <em>Casey</em>.  The Court has continued to embrace all the decisions <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>cited, decisions which recognize a constitutional right for an individual to make her own choices about "intimate relationships, the family," and contraception.  <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 857.  <em>Roe</em> and <em>Casey </em>have themselves formed the legal foundation for subsequent decisions protecting these profoundly personal choices.  As discussed earlier, the Court relied on <em>Casey </em>to hold that the Fourteenth Amendment protects same-sex intimate relationships.  See <em>Lawrence</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S., at 578; <em>supra</em>, at 23.  The Court later invoked the same set of precedents to accord constitutional recognition to same-sex marriage.  See <em>Obergefell</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S., at 665-666; <em>supra</em>, at 23.  In sum, <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>are inextricably interwoven with decades of precedent about the meaning of the Fourteenth Amendment.  See <em>supra</em>, at 21-24.  While the majority might wish it otherwise, <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em> are the very opposite of "&nbsp;'obsolete constitutional thinking.'&nbsp;"  <em>Agostini</em> v. <em>Felton</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 203, 236 (1997) (quoting <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 857).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Moreover, no subsequent factual developments have undermined <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  Women continue to experience unplanned pregnancies and unexpected developments in pregnancies.  Pregnancies continue to have enormous physical, social, and economic consequences.  Even an uncomplicated pregnancy imposes significant strain on the body, unavoidably involving significant physiological change and excruciating pain.  For some women, pregnancy and childbirth can mean life-altering physical ailments or even death.  Today, as noted earlier, the risks of carrying a pregnancy to term dwarf those of having an abortion.  See <em>supra</em>, at 22.  Experts estimate that a ban on abortions increases maternal mortality by 21 percent, with white women facing a 13 percent increase in maternal mortality while black women face a 33 percent increase.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 13" name="FRopinion1.13" href="#FNopinion1.13">13</a></sup>  Pregnancy and childbirth may also impose large-scale financial costs.  The majority briefly refers to arguments about changes in laws relating to healthcare coverage, pregnancy discrimination, and family leave.  See <em>ante</em>, at 33-34.  Many women, however, still do not have adequate healthcare coverage before and after pregnancy; and, even when insurance coverage is available, healthcare services may be far away.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 14" name="FRopinion1.14" href="#FNopinion1.14">14</a></sup>  Women also continue to face pregnancy discrimination that interferes with their ability to earn a living.  Paid family leave remains inaccessible to many who need it most.  Only 20 percent of private-sector workers have access to paid family leave, including a mere 8 percent of workers in the bottom quartile of wage earners.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 15" name="FRopinion1.15" href="#FNopinion1.15">15</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority briefly notes the growing prevalence of safe haven laws and demand for adoption, see <em>ante</em>, at 34, and nn. 45-46, but, to the degree that these are changes at all, they too are irrelevant.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 16" name="FRopinion1.16" href="#FNopinion1.16">16</a></sup>  Neither reduces the health risks or financial costs of going through pregnancy and childbirth.  Moreover, the choice to give up parental rights after giving birth is altogether different from the choice not to carry a pregnancy to term.  The reality is that few women denied an abortion will choose adoption.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 17" name="FRopinion1.17" href="#FNopinion1.17">17</a></sup>  The vast majority will continue, just as in <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>'s<em> </em>time, to shoulder the costs of childrearing.  Whether or not they choose to parent, they will experience the profound loss of autonomy and dignity that coerced pregnancy and birth always impose.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 18" name="FRopinion1.18" href="#FNopinion1.18">18</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Mississippi's own record illustrates how little facts on the ground have changed since <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>, notwithstanding the majority's supposed "modern developments."  <em>Ante</em>, at 33.  Sixty-two percent of pregnancies in Mississippi are unplanned, yet Mississippi does not require insurance to cover contraceptives and prohibits educators from demonstrating proper contraceptive use.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 19" name="FRopinion1.19" href="#FNopinion1.19">19</a></sup>  The State neither bans pregnancy discrimination nor requires provision of paid parental leave.  Brief for Yale Law School Information Society Project as <em>Amicus Curiae </em>13 (Brief for Yale Law School); Brief for National Women's Law Center et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae </em>32.  It has strict eligibility requirements for Medicaid and nutrition assistance, leaving many women and families without basic medical care or enough food.  See Brief for 547 Deans, Chairs, Scholars and Public Health Professionals et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 32-34 (Brief for 547 Deans).  Although 86 percent of pregnancy-related deaths in the State are due to postpartum complications, Mississippi rejected federal funding to provide a year's worth of Medicaid coverage to women after giving birth.  See Brief for Yale Law School 12-13.  Perhaps unsurprisingly, health outcomes in Mississippi are abysmal for both women and children.  Mississippi has the highest infant mortality rate in the country, and some of the highest rates for preterm birth, low birthweight, cesarean section, and maternal death.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 20" name="FRopinion1.20" href="#FNopinion1.20">20</a></sup>  It is approximately 75 times more dangerous for a woman in the State to carry a pregnancy to term than to have an abortion.  See Brief for 547 Deans 9-10.  We do not say that every State is Mississippi, and we are sure some have made gains since <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>in providing support for women and children.  But a state-by-state analysis by public health professionals shows that States with the most restrictive abortion policies also continue to invest the least in women's and children's health.  See Brief for 547 Deans 23-34.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The only notable change we can see since <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>cuts in favor of adhering to precedent: It is that American abortion law has become more and more aligned with other nations.  The majority, like the Mississippi Legislature, claims that the United States is an extreme outlier when it comes to abortion regulation.  See <em>ante</em>, at 6, and n.&nbsp;15.  The global trend, however, has been toward increased provision of legal and safe abortion care.  A number of countries, including New Zealand, the Netherlands, and Iceland, permit abortions up to a roughly similar time as <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>set.  See Brief for International and Comparative Legal Scholars as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 18-22.  Canada has decriminalized abortion at any point in a pregnancy.  See <em>id.</em>, at 13-15.  Most Western European countries impose restrictions on abortion after 12 to 14 weeks, but they often have liberal exceptions to those time limits, including to prevent harm to a woman's physical or mental health.  See <em>id.</em>, at 24-27; Brief for European Law Professors as <em>Amici Curiae </em>16-17, Appendix.  They also typically make access to early abortion easier, for example, by helping cover its cost.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 21" name="FRopinion1.21" href="#FNopinion1.21">21</a></sup>  Perhaps most notable, more than 50 countries around the world--in Asia, Latin America, Africa, and Europe--have expanded access to abortion in the past 25 years.  See Brief for International and Comparative Legal Scholars as <em>Amici Curiae </em>28-29.  In light of that worldwide liberalization of abortion laws, it is American States that will become international outliers after today.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, the majority can point to neither legal nor factual developments in support of its decision.  Nothing that has happened in this country or the world in recent decades undermines the core insight of <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  It continues to be true that, within the constraints those decisions established, a woman, not the government, should choose whether she will bear the burdens of pregnancy, childbirth, and parenting.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In support of its holding, see <em>ante</em>, at 40, the majority invokes two watershed cases overruling prior constitutional precedents: <em>West Coast Hotel Co.</em> v. <em>Parrish</em> and <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Board of Education</em>.  But those decisions, unlike today's, responded to changed law and to changed facts and attitudes that had taken hold throughout society.  As <em>Casey </em>recognized, the two cases are relevant only to show--by stark contrast--how unjustified overturning the right to choose is.  See 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 861-864.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>West Coast Hotel </em>overruled <em>Adkins</em> v. <em>Children's Hospital of D. C.</em>, 261 U.&nbsp;S. 525 (1923), and a whole line of cases beginning with <em>Lochner</em> v. <em>New York</em>, 198 U.&nbsp;S. 45 (1905).  <em>Adkins </em>had found a state minimum-wage law unconstitutional because, in the Court's view, the law interfered with a constitutional right to contract.  261 U.&nbsp;S., at 554-555.  But then the Great Depression hit, bringing with it unparalleled economic despair.  The experience undermined--in fact, it disproved--<em>Adkins</em>'s assumption that a wholly unregulated market could meet basic human needs.  As Justice Jackson (before becoming a Justice) wrote of that time: "The older world of <em>laissez faire</em> was recognized everywhere outside the Court to be dead."  The Struggle for Judicial Supremacy 85 (1941).  In <em>West Coast Hotel</em>, the Court caught up, recognizing through the lens of experience the flaws of existing legal doctrine.  See also <em>ante</em>, at 11 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts</small></span>, C.&nbsp;J., concurring in judgment).  The havoc the Depression had worked on ordinary Americans, the Court noted, was "common knowledge through the length and breadth of the land."  300 U.&nbsp;S., at 399.  The <em>laissez-faire</em> approach had led to "the exploiting of workers at wages so low as to be insufficient to meet the bare cost of living."  <em>Ibid.  </em>And since <em>Adkins </em>was decided, the law had also changed.  In several decisions, the Court had started to recognize the power of States to implement economic policies designed to enhance their citizens' economic well-being.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Nebbia</em> v. <em>New York</em>, 291 U.&nbsp;S. 502 (1934); <em>O'Gorman &amp; Young, Inc.</em> v. <em>Hartford Fire Ins. Co.</em>, 282 U.&nbsp;S. 251 (1931).  The statements in those decisions, <em>West Coast Hotel </em>explained, were "impossible to reconcile" with <em>Adkins</em>.  300 U.&nbsp;S., at 398.  There was no escaping the need for <em>Adkins </em>to go.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Brown</em> v. <em>Board of Education</em> overruled <em>Plessy</em> v. <em>Ferguson</em>, 163 U.&nbsp;S. 537 (1896), along with its doctrine of "separate but equal."  By 1954, decades of Jim Crow had made clear what <em>Plessy</em>'s turn of phrase actually meant: "inherent[&nbsp;] [in]equal[ity]."  <em>Brown</em>, 347 U.&nbsp;S., at 495.  Segregation was not, and could not ever be, consistent with the Reconstruction Amendments, ratified to give the former slaves full citizenship.  Whatever might have been thought in <em>Plessy</em>'s time, the <em>Brown </em>Court explained, both experience and "modern authority" showed the "detrimental effect[s]" of state-sanctioned segregation: It "affect[ed] [children's] hearts and minds in a way unlikely ever to be undone."  347 U.&nbsp;S., at 494.  By that point, too, the law had begun to reflect that understanding.  In a series of decisions, the Court had held unconstitutional public graduate schools' exclusion of black students.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Sweatt</em> v. <em>Painter</em>, 339 U.&nbsp;S. 629 (1950); <em>Sipuel</em> v. <em>Board of Regents of Univ. of Okla.</em>, 332 U.&nbsp;S. 631 (1948) (<em>per&nbsp;curiam</em>); <em>Missouri ex rel. Gaines</em> v. <em>Canada</em>, 305 U.&nbsp;S. 337 (1938).  The logic of those cases, <em>Brown </em>held, "appl[ied] with added force to children in grade and high schools."  347 U.&nbsp;S., at 494.  Changed facts and changed law required <em>Plessy</em>'s end.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority says that in recognizing those changes, we are implicitly supporting the half-century interlude between <em>Plessy </em>and <em>Brown</em>.  See <em>ante</em>, at 70.  That is not so.  First, if the <em>Brown </em>Court had used the majority's method of constitutional construction, it might not ever have overruled <em>Plessy</em>, whether 5 or 50 or 500 years later.  <em>Brown </em>thought that whether the ratification-era history supported desegregation was "[a]t best .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. inconclusive."  347 U.&nbsp;S., at 489.  But even setting that aside, we are not saying that a decision can <em>never</em> be overruled just because it is terribly wrong.  Take <em>West Virginia Bd. of Ed.</em> v. <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S. 624, which the majority also relies on.  See <em>ante</em>, at 40-41, 70.  That overruling took place just three years after the initial decision, before any notable reliance interests had developed.  It happened as well because individual Justices changed their minds, not because a new majority wanted to undo the decisions of their predecessors.  Both <em>Barnette </em>and <em>Brown</em>, moreover,<em> </em>share another feature setting them apart from the Court's ruling today.  They protected individual rights with a strong basis in the Constitution's most fundamental commitments; they did not, as the majority does here, take away a right that individuals have held, and relied on, for 50 years.  To take <em>that</em> action based on a new and bare majority's declaration that two Courts got the result egregiously wrong?  And to justify that action by reference to <em>Barnette</em>?  Or to<em> Brown</em>--a case in which the Chief Justice also wrote an (11-page) opinion in which the entire Court could speak with one voice?  These questions answer themselves.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Casey </em>itself addressed both <em>West Coast Hotel </em>and <em>Brown</em>, and found that neither supported <em>Roe</em>'s overruling.  In <em>West Coast Hotel</em>, <em>Casey </em>explained, "the facts of economic life" had proved "different from those previously assumed."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 862.  And even though "<em>Plessy </em>was wrong the day it was decided," the passage of time had made that ever more clear to ever more citizens: "Society's understanding of the facts" in 1954 was "fundamentally different" than in 1896.  <em>Id.</em>, at 863.  So the Court needed to reverse course.  "In constitutional adjudication as elsewhere in life, changed circumstances may impose new obligations."  <em>Id.</em>, at 864.  And because such dramatic change had occurred, the public could understand why the Court was acting.  "[T]he Nation could accept each decision" as a "response to the Court's constitutional duty."  <em>Ibid</em>.  But that would not be true of a reversal of <em>Roe</em>--"[b]ecause neither the factual underpinnings of <em>Roe</em>'s central holding nor our understanding of it has changed."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 864.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That is just as much so today, because <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>continue to reflect, not diverge from, broad trends in American society.  It is, of course, true that many Americans, including many women, opposed those decisions when issued and do so now as well.  Yet the fact remains: <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em> were the product of a profound and ongoing change in women's roles in the latter part of the 20th century.  Only a dozen years before <em>Roe</em>, the Court described women as "the center of home and family life," with "special responsibilities" that precluded their full legal status under the Constitution.  <em>Hoyt</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 368 U.&nbsp;S. 57, 62 (1961).  By 1973, when the Court decided <em>Roe</em>, fundamental social change was underway regarding the place of women--and the law had begun to follow.  See <em>Reed</em> v. <em>Reed</em>, 404 U.&nbsp;S. 71, 76 (1971) (recognizing that the Equal Protection Clause prohibits sex-based discrimination).  By 1992, when the Court decided <em>Casey</em>, the traditional view of a woman's role as only a wife and mother was "no longer consistent with our understanding of the family, the individual, or the Constitution."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 897; see <em>supra</em>, at 15, 23-24.  Under that charter, <em>Casey </em>understood, women must take their place as full and equal citizens.  And for that to happen, women must have control over their reproductive decisions.  Nothing since <em>Casey</em>--no changed law, no changed fact--has undermined that promise.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The reasons for retaining <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>gain further strength from the overwhelming reliance interests those decisions have created.  The Court adheres to precedent not just for institutional reasons, but because it recognizes that stability in the law is "an essential thread in the mantle of protection that the law affords the individual."  <em>Florida Dept. of Health and Rehabilitative Servs.</em> v. <em>Florida Nursing Home Assn.</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S. 147, 154 (1981) (Stevens, J., concurring).
