So when overruling precedent "would dislodge [individuals'] settled rights and expectations," <em>stare decisis</em> has "added force."  <em>Hilton</em> v. <em>South Carolina Public Railways Comm'n</em>, 502 U.&nbsp;S. 197, 202 (1991).  <em>Casey </em>understood that to deny individuals' reliance on <em>Roe </em>was to "refuse to face the fact[s]."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 856.  Today the majority refuses to face the facts.  "The most striking feature of the [majority] is the absence of any serious discussion" of how its ruling will affect women.  <em>Ante</em>, at 37.  By characterizing <em>Casey</em>'s<em> </em>reliance arguments as "generalized assertions about the national psyche," <em>ante</em>, at 64, it reveals how little it knows or cares about women's lives or about the suffering its decision will cause.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Casey</em>, the Court observed that for two decades individuals "have organized intimate relationships and made" significant life choices "in reliance on the availability of abortion in the event that contraception should fail."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 856.  Over another 30 years, that reliance has solidified.  For half a century now, in <em>Casey</em>'s words, "[t]he ability of women to participate equally in the economic and social life of the Nation has been facilitated by their ability to control their reproductive lives."  <em>Ibid.</em>; see <em>supra</em>, at 23-24.  Indeed, all women now of childbearing age have grown up expecting that they would be able to avail themselves of <em>Roe</em>'s<em> </em>and <em>Casey</em>'s protections.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The disruption of overturning <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>will therefore be profound.  Abortion is a common medical procedure and a familiar experience in women's lives.  About 18 percent of pregnancies in this country end in abortion, and about one quarter of American women will have an abortion before the age of 45.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 22" name="FRopinion1.22" href="#FNopinion1.22">22</a></sup>  Those numbers reflect the predictable and life-changing effects of carrying a pregnancy, giving birth, and becoming a parent.  As <em>Casey </em>understood, people today rely on their ability to control and time pregnancies when making countless life decisions: where to live, whether and how to invest in education or careers, how to allocate financial resources, and how to approach intimate and family relationships.  Women may count on abortion access for when contraception fails.  They may count on abortion access for when contraception cannot be used, for example, if they were raped.  They may count on abortion for when something changes in the midst of a pregnancy, whether it involves family or financial circumstances, unanticipated medical complications, or heartbreaking fetal diagnoses.  Taking away the right to abortion, as the majority does today, destroys all those individual plans and expectations.  In so doing, it diminishes women's opportunities to participate fully and equally in the Nation's political, social, and economic life.  See Brief for Economists as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 13 (showing that abortion availability has "large effects on women's education, labor force participation, occupations, and earnings" (footnotes omitted)).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority's response to these obvious points exists far from the reality American women actually live.  The majority proclaims that "&nbsp;'reproductive planning could take virtually immediate account of any sudden restoration of state authority to ban abortions.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ante</em>, at 64 (quoting <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 856).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 23" name="FRopinion1.23" href="#FNopinion1.23">23</a></sup>  The facts are: 45 percent of pregnancies in the United States are unplanned.  See Brief for 547 Deans 5.  Even the most effective contraceptives fail, and effective contraceptives are not universally accessible.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 24" name="FRopinion1.24" href="#FNopinion1.24">24</a></sup>  Not all sexual activity is consensual and not all contraceptive choices are made by the party who risks pregnancy.  See Brief for Legal Voice et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae </em>18-19.  The Mississippi law at issue here, for example, has no exception for rape or incest, even for underage women.  Finally, the majority ignores, as explained above, that some women decide to have an abortion because their circumstances change during a pregnancy.  See <em>supra</em>, at 49.  Human bodies care little for hopes and plans.  Events can occur after conception, from unexpected medical risks to changes in family circumstances, which profoundly alter what it means to carry a pregnancy to term.  In all these situations, women have expected that they will get to decide, perhaps in consultation with their families or doctors but free from state interference, whether to continue a pregnancy.  For those who will now have to undergo that pregnancy, the loss of <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>could be disastrous.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That is especially so for women without money.  When we "count[&nbsp;] the cost of [<em>Roe</em>'s] repudiation" on women who once relied on that decision, it is not hard to see where the greatest burden will fall.  <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 855.  In States that bar abortion, women of means will still be able to travel to obtain the services they need.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 25" name="FRopinion1.25" href="#FNopinion1.25">25</a></sup>  It is women who cannot afford to do so who will suffer most.  These are the women most likely to seek abortion care in the first place.  Women living below the federal poverty line experience unintended pregnancies at rates five times higher than higher income women do, and nearly half of women who seek abortion care live in households below the poverty line.  See Brief for 547 Deans 7; Brief for Abortion Funds and Practical Support Organizations as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 8 (Brief for Abortion Funds).  Even with <em>Roe</em>'s protection, these women face immense obstacles to raising the money needed to obtain abortion care early in their pregnancy.  See Brief for Abortion Funds 7-12.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 26" name="FRopinion1.26" href="#FNopinion1.26">26</a></sup>  After today, in States where legal abortions are not available, they will lose any ability to obtain safe, legal abortion care.  They will not have the money to make the trip necessary; or to obtain childcare for that time; or to take time off work.  Many will endure the costs and risks of pregnancy and giving birth against their wishes.  Others will turn in desperation to illegal and unsafe abortions.  They may lose not just their freedom, but their lives.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 27" name="FRopinion1.27" href="#FNopinion1.27">27</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, the expectation of reproductive control is integral to many women's identity and their place in the Nation.  See <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 856.  That expectation helps define a woman as an "equal citizen[&nbsp;]," with all the rights, privileges, and obligations that status entails.  <em>Gonzales</em>, 550 U.&nbsp;S., at 172 (Ginsburg, J., dissenting); see <em>supra</em>, at 23-24.  It reflects that she is an autonomous person, and that society and the law recognize her as such.  Like many constitutional rights, the right to choose situates a woman in relationship to others and to the government.  It helps define a sphere of freedom, in which a person has the capacity to make choices free of government control.  As <em>Casey </em>recognized, the right "order[s]" her "thinking" as well as her "living."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 856.  Beyond any individual choice about residence, or education, or career, her whole life reflects the control and authority that the right grants.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Withdrawing a woman's right to choose whether to continue a pregnancy does not mean that no choice is being made.  It means that a majority of today's Court has wrenched this choice from women and given it to the States.  To allow a State to exert control over one of "the most intimate and personal choices" a woman may make is not only to affect the course of her life, monumental as those effects might be.  <em>Id.</em>, at 851.  It is to alter her "views of [herself]" and her understanding of her "place[&nbsp;] in society" as someone with the recognized dignity and authority to make these choices.  <em>Id.</em>, at 856.  Women have relied on <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>in this way for 50 years.  Many have never known anything else.  When <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>disappear, the loss of power, control, and dignity will be immense.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's failure to perceive the whole swath of expectations <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>created reflects an impoverished view of reliance.  According to the majority, a reliance interest must be "very concrete," like those involving "property" or "contract."  <em>Ante</em>, at 64.  While many of this Court's cases addressing reliance have been in the "commercial context," <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 855, none holds that interests must be analogous to commercial ones to warrant <em>stare decisis</em> protection.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 28" name="FRopinion1.28" href="#FNopinion1.28">28</a></sup>  This unprecedented assertion is, at bottom, a radical claim to power.  By disclaiming any need to consider broad swaths of individuals' interests, the Court arrogates to itself the authority to overrule established legal principles without even acknowledging the costs of its decisions for the individuals who live under the law, costs that this Court's <em>stare decisis</em> doctrine instructs us to privilege when deciding whether to change course.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority claims that the reliance interests women have in <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>are too "intangible" for the Court to consider, even if it were inclined to do so.  <em>Ante</em>, at 65.<em>  </em>This is to ignore as judges what we know as men and women.  The interests women have in <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>are perfectly, viscerally concrete.  Countless women will now make different decisions about careers, education, relationships, and whether to try to become pregnant than they would have when <em>Roe </em>served as a backstop.  Other women will carry pregnancies to term, with all the costs and risk of harm that involves, when they would previously have chosen to obtain an abortion.  For millions of women, <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>have been critical in giving them control of their bodies and their lives.  Closing our eyes to the suffering today's decision will impose will not make that suffering disappear.  The majority cannot escape its obligation to "count[&nbsp;] the cost[s]" of its decision by invoking the "conflicting arguments" of "contending sides."  <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 855;<em> ante</em>, at 65.  <em>Stare decisis</em> requires that the Court calculate the costs of a decision's repudiation on those who have relied on the decision, not on those who have disavowed it.  See <em>Casey</em>,<em> </em>505 U.&nbsp;S., at 855.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;More broadly, the majority's approach to reliance cannot be reconciled with our Nation's understanding of constitutional rights.  The majority's insistence on a "concrete," economic showing would preclude a finding of reliance on a wide variety of decisions recognizing constitutional rights--such as the right to express opinions, or choose whom to marry, or decide how to educate children.  The Court, on the majority's logic, could transfer those choices to the State without having to consider a person's settled understanding that the law makes them hers.  That must be wrong.  All those rights, like the right to obtain an abortion, profoundly affect and, indeed, anchor individual lives.  To recognize that people have relied on these rights is not to dabble in abstractions, but to acknowledge some of the most "concrete" and familiar aspects of human life and liberty.  <em>Ante</em>, at 64.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;All those rights, like the one here, also have a societal dimension, because of the role constitutional liberties play in our structure of government.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Dickerson</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S., at 443 (recognizing that <em>Miranda </em>"warnings have become part of our national culture" in declining to overrule <em>Miranda </em>v. <em>Arizona</em>, 384 U.&nbsp;S. 436 (1966)).  Rescinding an individual right in its entirety and conferring it on the State, an action the Court takes today for the first time in history, affects all who have relied on our constitutional system of government and its structure of individual liberties protected from state oversight.  <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>have of course aroused controversy and provoked disagreement.  But the right those decisions conferred and reaffirmed is part of society's understanding of constitutional law and of how the Court has defined the liberty and equality that women are entitled to claim.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;After today, young women will come of age with fewer rights than their mothers and grandmothers had.  The majority accomplishes that result without so much as considering how women have relied on the right to choose or what it means to take that right away.  The majority's refusal even to consider the life-altering consequences of reversing <em>Roe</em> and <em>Casey</em> is a stunning indictment of its decision.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">D</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;One last consideration counsels against the majority's ruling: the very controversy surrounding <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  The majority accuses <em>Casey </em>of acting outside the bounds of the law to quell the conflict over abortion--of imposing an unprincipled "settlement" of the issue in an effort to end "national division."  <em>Ante</em>, at 67.  But that is not what <em>Casey </em>did.  As shown above, <em>Casey </em>applied traditional principles of <em>stare decisis</em>--which the majority today ignores--in reaffirming <em>Roe</em>.  <em>Casey </em>carefully assessed changed circumstances (none) and reliance interests (profound).  It considered every aspect of how <em>Roe</em>'s framework operated.  It adhered to the law in its analysis, and it reached the conclusion that the law required.  True enough that <em>Casey</em> took notice of the "national controversy" about abortion: The Court knew in 1992, as it did in 1973, that abortion was a "divisive issue."  <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 867-868; see <em>Roe</em>, 410 U.&nbsp;S., at 116.<em>  </em>But <em>Casey</em>'s reason for acknowledging public conflict was the exact opposite of what the majority insinuates.  <em>Casey</em> addressed the national controversy in order to emphasize how important it was, in that case of all cases, for the Court to stick to the law.  Would that today's majority had done likewise.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider how the majority itself summarizes this aspect of <em>Casey</em>:</p>
<blockquote><p>"The American people's belief in the rule of law would be shaken if they lost respect for this Court as an institution that decides important cases based on principle, not 'social and political pressures.'  There is a special danger that the public will perceive a decision as having been made for unprincipled reasons when the Court overrules a controversial 'watershed' decision, such as <em>Roe</em>.  A decision overruling <em>Roe </em>would be perceived as having been made 'under fire' and as a 'surrender to political pressure.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ante</em>, at 66-67 (citations omitted).</p>
</blockquote><p>That seems to us a good description.  And it seems to us right.  The majority responds (if we understand it correctly): well, yes, but we have to apply the law.  See <em>ante</em>, at 67.  To which <em>Casey </em>would have said: That is exactly the point.  Here, more than anywhere, the Court needs to apply the law--particularly the law of <em>stare decisis</em>.  Here, we know that citizens will continue to contest the Court's decision, because "[m]en and women of good conscience" deeply disagree about abortion.  <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S.,<em> </em>at 850.  When that contestation takes place--but when there is no legal basis for reversing course--the Court needs to be steadfast, to stand its ground.  That is what the rule of law requires.  And that is what respect for this Court depends on.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"The promise of constancy, once given" in so charged an environment, <em>Casey </em>explained, "binds its maker for as long as" the "understanding of the issue has not changed so fundamentally as to render the commitment obsolete."  <em>Id.</em>, at 868.  A breach of that promise is "nothing less than a breach of faith."  <em>Ibid.  </em>"[A]nd no Court that broke its faith with the people could sensibly expect credit for principle."  <em>Ibid.  </em>No Court breaking its faith in that way would <em>deserve</em> credit for principle.  As one of <em>Casey</em>'s authors wrote in another case, "Our legitimacy requires, above all, that we adhere to <em>stare decisis</em>" in "sensitive political contexts" where "partisan controversy abounds."  <em>Bush</em> v. <em>Vera</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 952, 985 (1996) (opinion of O'Connor, J.).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Justice Jackson once called a decision he dissented from a "loaded weapon," ready to hand for improper uses.  <em>Korematsu</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 323 U.&nbsp;S. 214, 246 (1944).  We fear that today's decision, departing from <em>stare decisis </em>for no legitimate reason, is its own loaded weapon.  Weakening <em>stare decisis</em> threatens to upend bedrock legal doctrines, far beyond any single decision.  Weakening <em>stare decisis </em>creates profound legal instability.  And as <em>Casey </em>recognized, weakening <em>stare decisis </em>in a hotly contested case like this one calls into question this Court's commitment to legal principle.  It makes the Court appear not restrained but aggressive, not modest but grasping.  In all those ways, today's decision takes aim, we fear, at the rule of law.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"Power, not reason, is the new currency of this Court's decisionmaking."  <em>Payne</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S., at 844 (Marshall, J., dissenting).  <em>Roe </em>has stood for fifty years.  <em>Casey</em>, a precedent about precedent specifically confirming <em>Roe</em>, has stood for thirty.  And the doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em>--a critical element of the rule of law--stands foursquare behind their continued existence.  The right those decisions established and preserved is embedded in our constitutional law, both originating in and leading to other rights protecting bodily integrity, personal autonomy, and family relationships.  The abortion right is also embedded in the lives of women--shaping their expectations, influencing their choices about relationships and work, supporting (as all reproductive rights do) their social and economic equality.  Since the right's recognition (and affirmation), nothing has changed to support what the majority does today.  Neither law nor facts nor attitudes have provided any new reasons to reach a different result than <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>did.  All that has changed is this Court.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Mississippi--and other States too--knew exactly what they were doing in ginning up new legal challenges to <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  The 15-week ban at issue here was enacted in 2018.  Other States quickly followed: Between 2019 and 2021, eight States banned abortion procedures after six to eight weeks of pregnancy, and three States enacted all-out bans.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 29" name="FRopinion1.29" href="#FNopinion1.29">29</a></sup>  Mississippi itself decided in 2019 that it had not gone far enough: The year after enacting the law under review, the State passed a 6-week restriction.  A state senator who championed both Mississippi laws said the obvious out loud.  "[A] lot of people thought," he explained, that "finally, we have" a conservative Court "and so now would be a good time to start testing the limits of <em>Roe</em>."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 30" name="FRopinion1.30" href="#FNopinion1.30">30</a></sup>  In its petition for certiorari, the State had exercised a smidgen of restraint.  It had urged the Court merely to roll back <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>, specifically assuring the Court that "the questions presented in this petition do not require the Court to overturn" those precedents.  Pet. for Cert. 5; see <em>ante</em>, at 5-6 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts</small></span>, C.&nbsp;J., concurring in judgment).  But as Mississippi grew ever more confident in its prospects, it resolved to go all in.  It urged the Court to overrule <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  Nothing but everything would be enough.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Earlier this Term, this Court signaled that Mississippi's stratagem would succeed.  Texas was one of the fistful of States to have recently banned abortions after six weeks of pregnancy.  It added to that "flagrantly unconstitutional" restriction an unprecedented scheme to "evade judicial scrutiny."  <em>Whole Woman's Health</em> v. <em>Jackson</em>, 594 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2021) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 1).  And five Justices acceded to that cynical maneuver.  They let Texas defy this Court's constitutional rulings, nullifying <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>ahead of schedule in the Nation's second largest State.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;And now the other shoe drops, courtesy of that same five-person majority.  (We believe that <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span>'s opinion is wrong too, but no one should think that there is not a large difference between upholding a 15-week ban on the grounds he does and allowing States to prohibit abortion from the time of conception.)  Now a new and bare majority of this Court--acting at practically the first moment possible--overrules <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  It converts a series of dissenting opinions expressing antipathy toward <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em> into a decision greenlighting even total abortion bans.  See <em>ante</em>, at<em> </em>57, 59, 63, and nn. 61-64 (relying on former dissents).  It eliminates a 50-year-old constitutional right that safeguards women's freedom and equal station.  It breaches a core rule-of-law principle, designed to promote constancy in the law.  In doing all of that, it places in jeopardy other rights, from contraception to same-sex intimacy and marriage.  And finally, it undermines the Court's legitimacy.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; <em>Casey </em>itself made the last point in explaining why it would not overrule <em>Roe</em>--though some members of its majority might not have joined <em>Roe </em>in the first instance.  Just as we did here, <em>Casey </em>explained the importance of <em>stare decisis</em>; the inappositeness of <em>West Coast Hotel </em>and <em>Brown</em>; the absence of any "changed circumstances" (or other reason) justifying the reversal of precedent.  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 864; see <em>supra</em>, at 30-33, 37-47.  "[T]he Court," <em>Casey </em>explained, "could not pretend" that overruling <em>Roe </em>had any "justification beyond a present doctrinal disposition to come out differently from the Court of 1973."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 864.<em>  </em>And to overrule for that reason?  Quoting Justice Stewart, <em>Casey </em>explained that to do so--to reverse prior law "upon a ground no firmer than a change in [the Court's] membership"--would invite the view that "this institution is little different from the two political branches of the Government."  <em>Ibid.  </em>No view, <em>Casey </em>thought, could do "more lasting injury to this Court and to the system of law which it is our abiding mission to serve."  <em>Ibid.  </em>For overruling <em>Roe</em>, <em>Casey </em>concluded, the Court would pay a "terrible price."  505 U.&nbsp;S., at 864.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Justices who wrote those words--O'Connor, Kennedy, and Souter--they were judges of wisdom.  They would not have won any contests for the kind of ideological purity some court watchers want Justices to deliver.  But if there were awards for Justices who left this Court better than they found it?  And who for that reason left this country better?  And the rule of law stronger?  Sign those Justices up.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;They knew that "the legitimacy of the Court [is] earned over time."  <em>Id.</em>, at 868.  They also would have recognized that it can be destroyed much more quickly.  They worked hard to avert that outcome in <em>Casey</em>.  The American public, they thought, should never conclude that its constitutional protections hung by a thread--that a new majority, adhering to a new "doctrinal school," could "by dint of numbers" alone expunge their rights.  <em>Id.</em>, at 864.<em>  </em>It is hard--no, it is impossible--to conclude that anything else has happened here.  One of us once said that "[i]t is not often in the law that so few have so quickly changed so much."  S. Breyer, Breaking the Promise of <em>Brown</em>: The Resegregation of America's Schools 30 (2022).  For all of us, in our time on this Court, that has never been more true than today.  In overruling <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>, this Court betrays its guiding principles.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;With sorrow--for this Court, but more, for the many millions of American women who have today lost a fundamental constitutional protection--we dissent.  </p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">APPENDIX</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This Appendix analyzes in full each of the 28 cases the majority says support today's decision to overrule <em>Roe </em>v. <em>Wade</em>, 410 U.&nbsp;S. 113 (1973),<em> </em>and <em>Planned Parenthood of Southeastern Pa. </em>v. <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 833 (1992).  As explained herein, the Court in each case relied on traditional <em>stare decisis</em> factors in overruling.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;A great many of the overrulings the majority cites involve a prior precedent that had been rendered out of step with or effectively abrogated by contemporary case law in light of intervening developments in the broader doctrine.  See <em>Ramos</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 590 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2020) (slip op., at 22) (holding the Sixth Amendment requires a unanimous jury verdict in state prosecutions for serious offenses, and overruling <em>Apodaca</em> v. <em>Oregon</em>, 406 U.&nbsp;S. 404 (1972), because "in the years since <em>Apodaca</em>, this Court ha[d] spoken inconsistently about its meaning" and had undercut its validity "on at least eight occasions"); <em>Ring</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S. 584, 608-609 (2002) (recognizing a Sixth Amendment right to have a jury find the aggravating factors necessary to impose a death sentence and, in so doing, rejecting <em>Walton</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 639 (1990), as overtaken by and irreconcilable with <em>Apprendi</em> v. <em>New Jersey</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 466 (2000)); <em>Agostini</em> v. <em>Felton</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 203, 235-236 (1997) (considering the Establishment Clause's constraint on government aid to religious instruction, and overruling <em>Aguilar</em> v. <em>Felton</em>, 473 U.&nbsp;S. 402 (1985), in light of several related doctrinal developments that had so undermined <em>Aguilar </em>and the assumption on which it rested as to render it no longer good law); <em>Batson</em> v. <em>Kentucky</em>, 476 U.&nbsp;S. 79, 93-96 (1986) (recognizing that a defendant may make a prima facie showing of purposeful racial discrimination in selection of a jury venire by relying solely on the facts in his case, and, based on subsequent developments in equal protection law, rejecting part of <em>Swain</em> v. <em>Alabama</em>, 380 U.&nbsp;S. 202 (1965), which had imposed a more demanding evidentiary burden); <em>Brandenburg</em> v. <em>Ohio</em>, 395 U.&nbsp;S. 444, 447-448 (1969) (<em>per curiam</em>) (holding that mere advocacy of violence is protected by the First Amendment, unless intended to incite it or produce imminent lawlessness, and rejecting the contrary rule in <em>Whitney</em> v. <em>California</em>, 274 U.&nbsp;S. 357 (1927), as having been "thoroughly discredited by later decisions"); <em>Katz</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 389 U.&nbsp;S. 347, 351, 353 (1967) (recognizing that the Fourth Amendment extends to material and communications that a person "seeks to preserve as private," and rejecting the more limited construction articulated in <em>Olmstead</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 277 U.&nbsp;S. 438 (1928), because "we have since departed from the narrow view on which that decision rested," and "the underpinnings of <em>Olmstead </em>.&nbsp;.&nbsp;. have been so eroded by our subsequent decisions that the 'trespass' doctrine there enunciated can no longer be regarded as controlling"); <em>Miranda</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 384 U.&nbsp;S. 436, 463-467, 479, n.&nbsp;48 (1966) (recognizing that the Fifth Amendment requires certain procedural safeguards for custodial interrogation, and rejecting <em>Crooker</em> v. <em>California</em>, 357 U.&nbsp;S. 433 (1958), and <em>Cicenia</em> v. <em>Lagay</em>, 357 U.&nbsp;S. 504 (1958), which had already been undermined by <em>Escobedo</em> v. <em>Illinois</em>, 378 U.&nbsp;S. 478 (1964)); <em>Malloy</em> v. <em>Hogan</em>, 378 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 6-9 (1964) (explaining that the Fifth Amendment privilege against "self-incrimination is also protected by the Fourteenth Amendment against abridgment by the States," and rejecting <em>Twining</em> v. <em>New Jersey</em>, 211 U.&nbsp;S. 78 (1908), in light of a "marked shift" in Fifth Amendment precedents that had "necessarily repudiated" the prior decision); <em>Gideon</em> v. <em>Wainwright</em>, 372 U.&nbsp;S. 335, 343-345 (1963) (acknowledging a right to counsel for indigent criminal defendants in state court under the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments, and overruling the earlier precedent failing to recognize such a right, <em>Betts</em> v. <em>Brady</em>, 316 U.&nbsp;S. 455 (1942));<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 31" name="FRopinion1.31" href="#FNopinion1.31">31</a></sup> <em>Smith</em> v. <em>Allwright</em>, 321 U.&nbsp;S. 649, 659-662 (1944) (recognizing all-white primaries are unconstitutional after reconsidering in light of "the unitary character of the electoral process" recognized in <em>United States</em> v. <em>Classic</em>, 313 U.&nbsp;S. 299 (1941), and overruling <em>Grovey</em> v. <em>Townsend</em>, 295 U.&nbsp;S. 45 (1935)); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Darby</em>, 312 U.&nbsp;S. 100, 115-117 (1941) (recognizing Congress's Commerce Clause power to regulate employment conditions and explaining as "inescapable" the "conclusion .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. that <em>Hammer</em> v. <em>Dagenhart</em>, [247 U.&nbsp;S. 251 (1918)]," and its contrary rule had "long since been" overtaken by precedent construing the Commerce Clause power more broadly); <em>Erie R. Co.</em> v. <em>Tompkins</em>, 304 U.&nbsp;S. 64, 78-80 (1938) (applying state substantive law in diversity actions in federal courts and overruling <em>Swift</em> v. <em>Tyson</em>, 16 Pet. 1 (1842), because an intervening decision had "made clear" the "fallacy underlying the rule").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Additional cases the majority cites involved fundamental factual changes that had undermined the basic premise of the prior precedent.  See <em>Citizens United</em> v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S. 310, 364 (2010) (expanding First Amendment protections for campaign-related speech and citing technological changes that undermined the distinctions of the earlier regime and made workarounds easy, and overruling <em>Austin</em> v. <em>Michigan Chamber of Commerce</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 652 (1990), and partially overruling <em>McConnell</em> v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 540 U.&nbsp;S. 93 (2003));<em> Crawford</em> v. <em>Washington</em>, 541 U.&nbsp;S. 36, 62-65 (2004) (expounding on the Sixth Amendment right to confront witnesses and rejecting the prior framework, based on its practical failing to keep out core testimonial evidence, and overruling <em>Ohio</em> v. <em>Roberts</em>, 448 U.&nbsp;S. 56 (1980)); <em>Mapp</em> v. <em>Ohio</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 643, 651-652 (1961) (holding that the exclusionary rule under the Fourth Amendment applies to the States, and overruling the contrary rule of <em>Wolf</em> v. <em>Colorado</em>, 338 U.&nbsp;S. 25 (1949), after considering and rejecting "the current validity of the factual grounds upon which <em>Wolf </em>was based").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Some cited overrulings involved <em>both </em>significant doctrinal developments <em>and</em> changed facts or understandings that had together undermined a basic premise of the prior decision.  See <em>Janus</em> v. <em>State, County, and Municipal Employees</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___, ___-___ (2018) (slip op., at 42, 47-49) (holding that requiring public-sector union dues from nonmembers violates the First Amendment, and overruling <em>Abood</em> v. <em>Detroit Bd. of Ed.</em>, 431 U.&nbsp;S. 209 (1977), based on "both factual and legal" developments that had "eroded the decision's underpinnings and left it an outlier among our First Amendment cases" (internal quotation marks omitted));<em> Obergefell</em> v. <em>Hodges</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 644, 659-663 (2015) (holding that the Fourteenth Amendment protects the right of same-sex couples to marry in light of doctrinal developments, as well as fundamentally changed social understanding);<em> Lawrence</em> v. <em>Texas</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 558, 572-578 (2003) (overruling <em>Bowers</em> v. <em>Hardwick</em>, 478 U.&nbsp;S. 186 (1986), after finding anti-sodomy laws to be inconsistent with the Fourteenth Amendment in light of developments in the legal doctrine, as well as changed social understanding of sexuality); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Scott</em>, 437 U.&nbsp;S. 82, 101 (1978) (overruling <em>United States</em> v. <em>Jenkins</em>, 420 U.&nbsp;S. 358 (1975), three years after it was decided, because of developments in the Court's double jeopardy case law, and because intervening practice had shown that government appeals from midtrial dismissals requested by the defendant were practicable, desirable, and consistent with double jeopardy values); <em>Craig</em> v. <em>Boren</em>, 429 U.&nbsp;S. 190, 197-199, 210, n. 23 (1976) (holding that sex-based classifications are subject to intermediate scrutiny under the Fourteenth Amendment's Equal Protection Clause, including because <em>Reed</em> v. <em>Reed</em>, 404 U.&nbsp;S. 71 (1971), and other equal protection cases and social changes had overtaken any "inconsistent" suggestion in <em>Goesaert</em> v. <em>Cleary</em>, 335 U.&nbsp;S. 464 (1948)); <em>Taylor</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 419 U.&nbsp;S. 522, 535-537 (1975) (recognizing as "a foregone conclusion from the pattern of some of the Court's cases over the past 30 years, as well as from legislative developments at both federal and state levels," that women could not be excluded from jury service, and explaining that the prior decision approving such practice, <em>Hoyt</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 368 U.&nbsp;S. 57 (1961), had been rendered inconsistent with equal protection jurisprudence).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Other overrulings occurred very close in time to the original decision so did not engender substantial reliance and could not be described as having been "embedded" as "part of our national culture."  <em>Dickerson </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 428, 443 (2000); see <em>Payne</em> v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808 (1991) (revising procedural rules of evidence that had barred admission of certain victim-impact evidence during the penalty phase of capital cases, and overruling <em>South Carolina</em> v. <em>Gathers</em>, 490 U.&nbsp;S. 805 (1989), and <em>Booth</em> v. <em>Maryland</em>, 482 U.&nbsp;S. 496 (1987), which had been decided two and four years prior, respectively); <em>Seminole Tribe of Fla.</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 44 (1996) (holding that Congress cannot abrogate state-sovereign immunity under its Article I commerce power, and rejecting the result in <em>Pennsylvania</em> v. <em>Union Gas Co.</em>, 491 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1989), seven years later; the decision in <em>Union Gas </em>never garnered a majority); <em>Garcia</em> v. <em>San Antonio Metropolitan Transit Authority</em>, 469 U.&nbsp;S. 528, 531 (1985) (holding that local governments are not constitutionally immune from federal employment laws, and overruling <em>National League of Cities</em> v. <em>Usery</em>, 426 U.&nbsp;S. 833 (1976), after "eight years" of experience under that regime showed <em>Usery</em>'s standard was unworkable and, in practice, undermined the federalism principles the decision sought to protect).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The rest of the cited cases were relatively minor in their effect, modifying part or an application of a prior precedent's test or analysis.  See <em>Montejo</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. 778 (2009) (citing workability and practical concerns with additional layers of prophylactic procedural safeguards for defendants' right to counsel, as had been enshrined in <em>Michigan</em> v. <em>Jackson</em>, 475 U.&nbsp;S. 625 (1986)); <em>Illinois</em> v. <em>Gates</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 213, 227-228 (1983) (replacing a two-pronged test under <em>Aguilar</em> v. <em>Texas</em>, 378 U.&nbsp;S. 108 (1964), and <em>Spinelli</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 393 U.&nbsp;S. 410 (1969), in favor of a traditional totality-of-the-circumstances approach to evaluate probable cause for issuance of a warrant); <em>Wesberry</em> v. <em>Sanders</em>, 376 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 4 (1964), and <em>Baker</em> v. <em>Carr</em>, 369 U.&nbsp;S. 186, 202 (1962) (clarifying that the "political question" passage of the minority opinion in <em>Colegrove</em> v. <em>Green</em>, 328 U.&nbsp;S. 549 (1946), was not controlling law).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, none of the cases the majority cites is analogous to today's decision to overrule 50- and 30-year-old watershed constitutional precedents that remain unweakened by any changes of law or fact.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;That is true regardless of whether we look to the Amendment's Due Process Clause or its Privileges or Immunities Clause.  Some scholars and Justices have maintained that the Privileges or Immunities Clause is the provision of the Fourteenth Amendment that guarantees substantive rights.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>McDonald</em> v. <em>Chicago</em>, 561 U.&nbsp;S. 742, 813-850 (2010) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., concurring in part and concurring in judgment); <em>Duncan</em>, 391 U.&nbsp;S., at 165-166 (Black, J., concurring);<em> </em>A. Amar, Bill of Rights: Creation and Reconstruction 163-180 (1998) (Amar); J. Ely, Democracy and Distrust 22-30 (1980); 2 W. Crosskey, Politics and the Constitution in the History of the United States 1089-1095 (1953).  But even on that view, such a right would need to be rooted in the Nation's history and tradition.  See <em>Corfield</em> v. <em>Coryell</em>, 6 F. Cas. 546, 551-552 (No. 3,230) (CC ED Pa. 1823) (describing unenumerated rights under the Privileges and Immunities Clause, Art. IV, §2, as those "fundamental" rights "which have, at all times, been enjoyed by the citizens of the several states"); Amar 176 (relying on <em>Corfield </em>to interpret the Privileges or Immunities Clause); cf. <em>McDonald</em>, 561<em> </em>U.&nbsp;S., at 819-820, 832, 854 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.) (reserving the question whether the Privileges or Immunities Clause protects "any rights besides those enumerated in the Constitution").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See R. Lucas, Federal Constitutional Limitations on the Enforcement and Administration of State Abortion Statutes, 46 N. C. L. Rev. 730 (1968) (Lucas); see also D. Garrow, Liberty and Sexuality 334-335 (1994) (Garrow) (stating that Lucas was "undeniably the first person to fully articulate on paper" the argument that "a woman's right to choose abortion was a fundamental individual freedom protected by the U.&nbsp;S. Constitution's guarantee of personal liberty").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The exact meaning of "quickening" is subject to some debate.  Compare Brief for Scholars of Jurisprudence as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 12-14, and  n. 32 (emphasis deleted) ("&nbsp;'a quick child'&nbsp;" meant simply a "live" child, and under the era's outdated knowledge of embryology, a fetus was thought to become "quick" at around the sixth week of pregnancy), with Brief for American Historical Association et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 6, n.&nbsp;2 ("quick" and "quickening" consistently meant "the woman's perception of fetal movement").  We need not wade into this debate.  First, it suffices for present purposes to show that abortion was criminal by <em>at least </em>the<em> </em>16th or 18th week of pregnancy.  Second, as we will show, during the relevant period--<em>i.e., </em>the period surrounding the enactment of the Fourteenth Amendment--the quickening distinction was abandoned as States criminalized abortion at all stages of pregnancy.  See <em>infra</em>, at 21-25.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Even before Bracton's time, English law imposed punishment for the killing of a fetus.  See Leges Henrici Primi 222-223 (L. Downer ed. 1972) (imposing penalty for any abortion and treating a woman who aborted a "quick" child "as if she were a murderess").</p>

Greaves, Crimes and Misdemeanors 540 (5th ed. 1845) ("So where a person gave medicine to a woman to procure an abortion, and where a person put skewers into the woman for the same purpose, by which in both cases the women were killed, these acts were clearly held to be murder" (footnotes omitted)); 1 E. East, Pleas of the Crown 230 (1803) (similar).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 32" name="FNopinion1.32" href="#FRopinion1.32">32</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Mitchell</em> v. <em>Commonwealth</em>, 78 Ky. 204, 209-210 (1879) (acknowledging the common-law rule but arguing that "the law should punish abortions and miscarriages, willfully produced, at any time during the period of gestation"); <em>Mills</em> v. <em>Commonwealth</em>, 13 Pa., 631, 633 (1850) (the quickening rule "never ought to have been the law anywhere"); J. <br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 34" name="FNopinion1.34" href="#FRopinion1.34">34</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Some scholars assert that only 27 States prohibited abortion at all stages.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Dellapenna 315; Witherspoon 34-35, and n.&nbsp;15.  Those scholars appear to have overlooked Rhode Island, which criminalized abortion at all stages in 1861.  See Acts and Resolves R.&nbsp;I. 1861, ch. 371, §1, p. 133 (criminalizing the attempt to "procure the miscarriage" of "any pregnant woman" or "any woman supposed by such person to be pregnant," without mention of quickening).  The <em>amicus </em>brief for the American Historical Association asserts that only 26 States prohibited abortion at all stages, but that brief incorrectly excludes West Virginia and Nebraska from its count.  Compare Brief for American Historical Association 27-28 (citing Quay), with Appendix A, <em>infra</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNopinion1.35" href="#FRopinion1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The statutes of three States (Massachusetts, New Jersey, and Pennsylvania) prohibited abortions performed "unlawfully" or "without lawful justification."  <em>Roe</em>, 410 U.&nbsp;S., at 139 (internal quotation marks omitted).  In Massachusetts, case law held that abortion was allowed when, according to the judgment of physicians in the relevant community, the procedure was necessary to preserve the woman's life or her physical or emotional health.  <em>Commonwealth </em>v<em>. Wheeler</em>, 315 Mass. 394, 395, 53 N.&nbsp;E. 2d 4, 5 (1944).  In the other two States, however, there is no clear support in case law for the proposition that abortion was lawful where the mother's life was not at risk.  See <em>State </em>v<em>. Brandenberg, </em>137 N.&nbsp;J.&nbsp;L. 124, 58 A. 2d 709 (1948); <em>Commonwealth </em>v<em>. Trombetta</em>, 131 Pa. Super. 487, 200 A. 107 (1938).</p>
<p>Statutes in the two remaining jurisdictions (the District of Columbia and Alabama) permitted "abortion to preserve the mother's health."  <em>Roe</em>, 410 U.&nbsp;S., at 139.  Case law in those jurisdictions does not clarify the breadth of these exceptions.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 36" name="FNopinion1.36" href="#FRopinion1.36">36</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See 410 U.&nbsp;S., at 154-155 (collecting cases decided between 1970 and 1973); C. Means, The Phoenix of Abortional Freedom: Is a Penumbral or Ninth-Amendment Right About To Arise From the Nineteenth-Century Legislative Ashes of a Fourteenth-Century Common-Law Liberty? 17 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;L. Forum 335, 337-339 (1971) (Means II); C. Means, The Law of New York Concerning Abortion and the Status of the Foetus, 1664-1968: A Case of Cessation of Constitutionality, 14 N.&nbsp;Y.&nbsp;L. Forum 411 (1968) (Means I); Lucas 730.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 37" name="FNopinion1.37" href="#FRopinion1.37">37</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See 410 U.&nbsp;S., at 136, n. 26 (citing Means II); 410 U.&nbsp;S., at 132-133, n. 21 (citing Means I).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 38" name="FNopinion1.38" href="#FRopinion1.38">38</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For critiques of Means's work, see, <em>e.g.</em>, Dellapenna 143-152, 325-331; Keown 3-12; J. Finnis, "Shameless Acts" in Colorado: Abuse of Scholarship in Constitutional Cases, 7 Academic Questions 10, 11-12 (1994); R. Destro, Abortion and the Constitution: The Need for a Life-Protective Amendment, 63 Cal. L. Rev. 1250, 1267-1282 (1975); R. Byrn, An American Tragedy: The Supreme Court on Abortion, 41 Ford. L. Rev. 807, 814-829 (1973).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 39" name="FNopinion1.39" href="#FRopinion1.39">39</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Garrow 500-501, and n. 41 (internal quotation marks omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 40" name="FNopinion1.40" href="#FRopinion1.40">40</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In any event, <em>Roe</em>, <em>Casey</em>, and other related abortion decisions imposed substantial restrictions on a State's capacity to regulate abortions performed after quickening.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>June Medical Services L.&nbsp;L.&nbsp;C.</em> v. <em>Russo</em>, 591 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2020) (holding a law requiring doctors performing abortions to secure admitting privileges to be unconstitutional); <em>Whole Woman's Health</em> v. <em>Hellerstedt</em>, 579 U.&nbsp;S. 582 (2016) (similar); <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 846 (declaring that prohibitions on "abortion before viability" are unconstitutional); <em>id.</em>, at<em> </em>887-898 (holding that a spousal notification provision was unconstitutional).  In addition, <em>Doe </em>v.<em> Bolton</em>, 410 U.&nbsp;S. 179 (1973), has been interpreted by some to protect a broad right to obtain an abortion at any stage of pregnancy provided that a physician is willing to certify that it is needed due to a woman's "emotional" needs or "familial" concerns.  <em>Id</em>., at 192.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Women's Medical Professional Corp. </em>v.<em> Voinovich</em>, 130 F. 3d 187, 209 (CA6 1997), cert. denied, 523 U.&nbsp;S. 1036 (1998); but see <em>id</em>., at 1039 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., dissenting from denial of certiorari).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 41" name="FNopinion1.41" href="#FRopinion1.41">41</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Other <em>amicus</em> briefs present arguments about the motives of proponents of liberal access to abortion.  They note that some such supporters have been motivated by a desire to suppress the size of the AfricanAmerican population.  See Brief for African-American Organization et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 14-21; see also <em>Box</em> v. <em>Planned Parenthood of Ind. and Ky., Inc.</em>, 587 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2019) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., concurring) (slip op., at 1-4).  And it is beyond dispute that <em>Roe</em> has had that demographic effect.  A highly disproportionate percentage of aborted fetuses are Black.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Dept. of Health and Human Servs., Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), K. Kortsmit et&nbsp;al., Abortion Surveillance--United States, 2019, 70 Morbidity and Mortality Report, Surveillance Summaries, p. 20 (Nov. 26, 2021) (Table 6).  For our part, we do not question the motives of either those who have supported or those who have opposed laws restricting abortions.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 42" name="FNopinion1.42" href="#FRopinion1.42">42</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g</em>., Pregnancy Discrimination Act, 92 Stat. 2076, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. §2000e(k) (federal law prohibiting pregnancy discrimination in employment); Dept. of Labor, Women's Bureau, Employment Protections for Workers Who Are Pregnant or Nursing, https://www.dol.gov/agencies/ wb/pregnant-nursing-employment-protections (showing that 46 States and the District of Columbia have employment protections against pregnancy discrimination).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 43" name="FNopinion1.43" href="#FRopinion1.43">43</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, Family and Medical Leave Act of 1993, 107 Stat. 9, 29 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. §2612 (federal law guaranteeing employment leave for pregnancy and birth); Bureau of Labor Statistics, Access to Paid and Unpaid Family Leave in 2018, https://www.bls.gov/opub/ted/2019/access-to-paid-and-unpaid-family-leave-in-2018.htm (showing that 89 percent of civilian workers had access to unpaid family leave in 2018).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 44" name="FNopinion1.44" href="#FRopinion1.44">44</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Affordable Care Act (ACA) requires non-grandfathered health plans in the individual and small group markets to cover certain essential health benefits, which include maternity and newborn care.  See 124 Stat. 163, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. §18022(b)(1)(D).  The ACA also prohibits annual limits, see §300gg-11, and limits annual cost-sharing obligations on such benefits, §18022(c).  State Medicaid plans must provide coverage for  pregnancy-related services--including, but not limited to, prenatal care, delivery, and postpartum care--as well as services for other conditions that might complicate the pregnancy.  42 CFR §§440.210(a)(2)(i)-(ii) (2020).  State Medicaid plans are also prohibited from imposing deductions, cost-sharing, or similar charges for pregnancy-related services for pregnant women.  42&nbsp;U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. §§1396o(a)(2)(B), (b)(2)(B).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 45" name="FNopinion1.45" href="#FRopinion1.45">45</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Since <em>Casey</em>, all 50 States and the District of Columbia have enacted such laws.  Dept. of Health and Human Servs., Children's Bureau, Infant Safe Haven Laws 1-2 (2016), https://www.childwelfare.gov/pubPDFs/ safehaven.pdf (noting that safe haven laws began in Texas in 1999).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 46" name="FNopinion1.46" href="#FRopinion1.46">46</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, CDC, Adoption Experiences of Women and Men and Demand for Children To Adopt by Women 18-44 Years of Age in the United States 16 (Aug. 2008) ("[N]early 1 million women were seeking to adopt children in 2002 (<em>i.e</em>., they were in demand for a child), whereas the domestic supply of infants relinquished at birth or within the first month of life and available to be adopted had become virtually nonexistent"); CDC, National Center for Health Statistics, Adoption and Nonbiological Parenting, https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/nsfg/key_statistics/a-keystat.htm# adoption (showing that approximately 3.1 million women between the ages of 18-49 had ever "[t]aken steps to adopt a child" based on data collected from 2015-2019).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 48" name="FNopinion1.48" href="#FRopinion1.48">48</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Obergefell</em> v. <em>Hodges</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 644 (2015) (right to same-sex marriage), overruling <em>Baker </em>v. <em>Nelson</em>, 409 U.&nbsp;S. 810 (1972); <em>Citizens United</em> v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S. 310 (2010) (right to engage in campaign-related speech), overruling <em>Austin </em>v. <em>Michigan Chamber of Commerce</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 652 (1990), and partially overruling <em>McConnell </em>v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 540 U.&nbsp;S. 93 (2003); <em>Montejo</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. 778 (2009) (Sixth Amendment right to counsel), overruling <em>Michigan </em>v. <em>Jackson</em>, 475 U.&nbsp;S. 625 (1986); <em>Crawford</em> v. <em>Washington</em>, 541 U.&nbsp;S. 36 (2004) (Sixth Amendment right to confront witnesses), overruling <em>Ohio </em>v. <em>Roberts</em>, 448 U.&nbsp;S. 56 (1980); <em>Lawrence</em> v. <em>Texas</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 558 (2003) (right to engage in consensual, same-sex intimacy in one's home), overruling <em>Bowers </em>v. <em>Hardwick</em>, 478 U.&nbsp;S. 186 (1986); <em>Ring</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S. 584 (2002) (Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial in capital prosecutions), overruling <em>Walton </em>v. <em>Arizona</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 639 (1990); <em>Agostini</em> v. <em>Felton</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 203 (1997) (evaluating whether government aid violates the Establishment Clause), overruling <em>Aguilar </em>v. <em>Felton</em>, 473 U.&nbsp;S. 402 (1985), and <em>School Dist. of Grand Rapids </em>v. <em>Ball</em>, 473 U.&nbsp;S. 373 (1985); <em>Seminole Tribe of Fla.</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 44 (1996) (lack of congressional power under the Indian Commerce Clause to abrogate States' Eleventh Amendment immunity), overruling <em>Pennsylvania </em>v. <em>Union Gas Co.</em>, 491 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1989); <em>Payne</em> v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808 (1991) (the Eighth Amendment does not erect a <em>per se</em> bar to the admission of victim impact evidence during the penalty phase of a capital trial), overruling <em>Booth </em>v. <em>Maryland</em>, 482 U.&nbsp;S. 496 (1987), and <em>South Carolina </em>v. <em>Gathers</em>, 490 U.&nbsp;S. 805 (1989); <em>Batson</em> v. <em>Kentucky</em>, 476 U.&nbsp;S. 79 (1986) (the Equal Protection Clause guarantees the defendant that the State will not exclude members of his race from the jury venire on account of race), overruling <em>Swain </em>v. <em>Alabama</em>, 380 U.&nbsp;S. 202 (1965); <em>Garcia</em> v. <em>San Antonio Metropolitan Transit Authority</em>, 469 U.&nbsp;S. 528, 530 (1985) (rejecting the principle that the Commerce Clause does not empower Congress to enforce requirements, such as minimum wage laws, against the States "&nbsp;'in areas of traditional governmental functions'&nbsp;"), overruling <em>National League of Cities </em>v. <em>Usery</em>, 426 U.&nbsp;S. 833 (1976); <em>Illinois</em> v. <em>Gates</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 213 (1983) (the Fourth Amendment requires a totality of the circumstances approach for determining whether an informant's tip establishes probable cause), overruling <em>Aguilar </em>v. <em>Texas</em>, 378 U.&nbsp;S. 108 (1964), and <em>Spinelli </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 393 U.&nbsp;S. 410 (1969); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Scott</em>, 437 U.&nbsp;S. 82 (1978) (the Double Jeopardy Clause does not apply to Government appeals from orders granting defense motions to terminate a trial before verdict), overruling <em>United States </em>v. <em>Jenkins</em>, 420 U.&nbsp;S. 358 (1975); <em>Craig</em> v. <em>Boren</em>, 429 U.&nbsp;S. 190 (1976) (gender-based classifications are subject to intermediate scrutiny under the Equal Protection Clause), overruling <em>Goesaert </em>v. <em>Cleary</em>, 335 U.&nbsp;S. 464 (1948); <em>Taylor</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 419 U.&nbsp;S. 522 (1975) (jury system which operates to exclude women from jury service violates the defendant's Sixth and Fourteenth Amendment right to an impartial jury), overruling <em>Hoyt </em>v. <em>Florida</em>, 368 U.&nbsp;S. 57 (1961); <em>Brandenburg</em> v. <em>Ohio</em>, 395 U.&nbsp;S. 444 (1969) (<em>per curiam</em>) (the mere advocacy of violence is protected under the First Amendment unless it is directed to incite or produce imminent lawless action), overruling <em>Whitney </em>v. <em>California</em>, 274 U.&nbsp;S. 357 (1927); <em>Katz</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 389 U.&nbsp;S. 347, 351 (1967) (Fourth Amendment "protects people, not places," and extends to what a person "seeks to preserve as private"), overruling <em>Olmstead </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 277 U.&nbsp;S. 438 (1928), and <em>Goldman </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 316 U.&nbsp;S. 129 (1942); <em>Miranda</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 384 U.&nbsp;S. 436 (1966) (procedural safeguards to protect the Fifth Amendment privilege against self-incrimination), overruling <em>Crooker </em>v. <em>California</em>, 357 U.&nbsp;S. 433 (1958), and <em>Cicenia </em>v. <em>Lagay</em>, 357 U.&nbsp;S. 504 (1958); <em>Malloy</em> v. <em>Hogan</em>, 378 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1964) (the Fifth Amendment privilege against self-incrimination is also protected by the Fourteenth Amendment against abridgment by the States), overruling <em>Twining </em>v. <em>New Jersey</em>, 211 U.&nbsp;S. 78 (1908), and <em>Adamson </em>v. <em>California</em>, 332 U.&nbsp;S. 46 (1947); <em>Wesberry</em> v. <em>Sanders</em>, 376 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 7-8 (1964) (congressional districts should be apportioned so that "as nearly as is practicable one man's vote in a congressional election is to be worth as much as another's"), overruling in effect <em>Colegrove </em>v. <em>Green</em>, 328 U.&nbsp;S. 549 (1946); <em>Gideon</em> v. <em>Wainwright</em>, 372 U.&nbsp;S. 335 (1963) (right to counsel for indigent defendant in a criminal prosecution in state court under the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments), overruling <em>Betts </em>v. <em>Brady</em>, 316 U.&nbsp;S. 455 (1942); <em>Baker</em> v. <em>Carr</em>, 369 U.&nbsp;S. 186 (1962) (federal courts have jurisdiction to consider constitutional challenges to state redistricting plans), effectively overruling in part <em>Colegrove</em>, 328 U.&nbsp;S. 549; <em>Mapp</em> v. <em>Ohio</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 643 (1961) (the exclusionary rule regarding the inadmissibility of evidence obtained in violation of the Fourth Amendment applies to the States), overruling <em>Wolf </em>v. <em>Colorado</em>, 338 U.&nbsp;S. 25 (1949); <em>Smith</em> v. <em>Allwright</em>, 321 U.&nbsp;S. 649 (1944) (racial restrictions on the right to vote in primary elections violates the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment), overruling <em>Grovey </em>v. <em>Townsend</em>, 295 U.&nbsp;S. 45 (1935); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Darby</em>, 312 U.&nbsp;S. 100 (1941) (congressional power to regulate employment conditions under the Commerce Clause), overruling <em>Hammer </em>v. <em>Dagenhart</em>, 247 U.&nbsp;S. 251 (1918); <em>Erie R. Co.</em> v. <em>Tompkins</em>, 304 U.&nbsp;S. 64 (1938) (Congress does not have the power to declare substantive rules of common law; a federal court sitting in diversity jurisdiction must apply the substantive state law), overruling <em>Swift </em>v. <em>Tyson</em>, 16 Pet. 1 (1842).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 50" name="FNopinion1.50" href="#FRopinion1.50">50</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, P. Singer, Rethinking Life &amp; Death 218 (1994) (defining a person as "a being with awareness of her or his own existence over time, and the capacity to have wants and plans for the future"); B. Steinbock, Life Before Birth: The Moral and Legal Status of Embryos and Fetuses 9-13 (1992) (arguing that "the possession of interests is both necessary and sufficient for moral status" and that the "capacity for conscious awareness is a necessary condition for the possession of interests" (emphasis deleted)); M. Warren, On the Moral and Legal Status of Abortion, 57 The Monist 1, 5 (1973) (arguing that, to qualify as a person, a being must have at least one of five traits that are "central to the concept of personhood": (1) "consciousness (of objects and events external and/or internal to the being), and in particular the capacity to feel pain"; (2) "reasoning (the developed capacity to solve new and relatively complex problems)"; (3) "self-motivated activity (activity which is relatively independent of either genetic or direct external control)"; (4) "the capacity to communicate, by whatever means, messages of an indefinite variety of types"; and (5) "the presence of self-concepts, and self-awareness, either individual or racial, or both" (emphasis deleted)); M. Tooley, Abortion &amp; Infanticide, 2 Philosophy &amp; Pub. Affairs 37, 49 (Autumn 1972) (arguing that "having a right to life presupposes that one is capable of desiring to continue existing as a subject of experiences and other mental states").</p>

Baker, The Incubator and the Medical Discovery of the Premature Infant, J. <br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 59" name="FNopinion1.59" href="#FRopinion1.59">59</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Memphis Center for Reproductive Health</em> v. <em>Slatery</em>, 14 F.&nbsp;4th 409, 451 (CA6 2021) (Thapar, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part); <em>Preterm-Cleveland</em>, 994 F.&nbsp;3d, at 524; <em>Planned Parenthood of Ind. &amp; Ky., Inc.</em> v. <em>Commissioner of Ind. State Dept. of Health</em>, 888 F.&nbsp;3d 300, 313 (CA7 2018) (Manion, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part); <em>Planned Parenthood of Ind. &amp; Ky., Inc.</em> v. <em>Box</em>, 949 F.&nbsp;3d 997, 999 (CA7 2019) (Easterbrook, J., concurring in denial of reh'g en banc) ("How much burden is 'undue' is a matter of judgment, which depends on what the burden would be .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. and whether that burden is excessive (a matter of weighing costs against benefits, which one judge is apt to do differently from another, and which judges as a group are apt to do differently from state legislators)"); <em>National Abortion Federation </em>v. <em>Gonzales</em>, 437 F.&nbsp;3d 278, 290-296 (CA2 2006) (Walker, C.&nbsp;J., concurring); <em>Planned Parenthood of Rocky Mountains Servs. Corp. </em>v. <em>Owens</em>, 287 F.&nbsp;3d 910, 931 (CA10 2002) (Baldock, J., dissenting).</p>
