<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>*<em>Griswold </em>v. <em>Connecticut</em> purported not to rely on the Due Process Clause, but rather reasoned "that specific guarantees in the Bill of Rights"--including rights enumerated in the First, Third, Fourth, Fifth, and Ninth Amendments--"have penumbras, formed by emanations," that create "zones of privacy."  381 U.&nbsp;S., at 484.  Since <em>Griswold, </em>the Court, perhaps recognizing the facial absurdity of <em>Griswold</em>'s penumbral argument, has characterized the decision as one rooted in substantive due process.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Obergefell </em>v. <em>Hodges</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 644, 663 (2015); <em>Washington </em>v. <em>Glucksberg</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 702, 720 (1997).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court's opinion today also recounts the pre-constitutional  common-law history in England.  That English history supplies background information on the issue of abortion.  As I see it, the dispositive point in analyzing American history and tradition for purposes of the Fourteenth Amendment inquiry is that abortion was largely prohibited in most American States as of 1868 when the Fourteenth Amendment was ratified, and that abortion remained largely prohibited in most American States until <em>Roe</em> was decided in 1973.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In his dissent in <em>Roe</em>, Justice Rehnquist indicated that an exception to a State's restriction on abortion would be constitutionally required when an abortion is necessary to save the life of the mother.  See <em>Roe</em> v. <em>Wade</em>, 410 U.&nbsp;S. 113, 173 (1973).  Abortion statutes traditionally and currently provide for an exception when an abortion is necessary to protect the life of the mother.  Some statutes also provide other exceptions.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I also agree with the Court's conclusion today with respect to reliance.  Broad notions of societal reliance have been invoked in support of <em>Roe</em>, but the Court has not analyzed reliance in that way in the past.  For example, American businesses and workers relied on <em>Lochner</em> v. <em>New York</em>, 198 U.&nbsp;S. 45 (1905), and <em>Adkins </em>v.<em> Children's Hospital of D. C.</em>, 261 U.&nbsp;S. 525 (1923), to construct a laissez-faire economy that was free of substantial regulation.  In <em>West Coast Hotel Co.</em> v. <em>Parrish</em>, 300 U.&nbsp;S. 379 (1937),<em> </em>the Court nonetheless overruled <em>Adkins </em>and in effect <em>Lochner</em>.  An entire region of the country relied on <em>Plessy</em> v. <em>Ferguson</em>, 163 U.&nbsp;S. 537 (1896), to enforce a system of racial segregation.  In <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Board of Education</em>, 347 U.&nbsp;S. 483 (1954),<em> </em>the Court overruled <em>Plessy</em>.  Much of American society was built around the traditional view of marriage that was upheld in <em>Baker</em> v. <em>Nelson</em>, 409 U.&nbsp;S. 810 (1972), and that was reflected in laws ranging from tax laws to estate laws to family laws.  In <em>Obergefell</em> v. <em>Hodges</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 644 (2015),<em> </em>the Court nonetheless overruled <em>Baker</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As the Court today notes, <em>Casey</em>'s approach to <em>stare decisis</em> pointed in two directions.  <em>Casey</em> reaffirmed <em>Roe</em>'s viability line, but it expressly overruled the <em>Roe</em> trimester framework and also expressly overruled two landmark post-<em>Roe</em> abortion cases--<em>Akron</em> v. <em>Akron</em> <em>Center</em> <em>for</em> <em>Reproductive</em> <em>Health</em>, <em>Inc</em>., 462 U.&nbsp;S. 416 (1983), and <em>Thornburgh</em> v. <em>American</em> <em>College</em> <em>of</em> <em>Obstetricians</em> <em>and</em> <em>Gynecologists</em>, 476 U.&nbsp;S. 747 (1986).  See <em>Casey</em>,<em> </em>505 U.&nbsp;S., at 870, 872−873, 878−879, 882.  <em>Casey</em> itself thus directly contradicts any notion of absolute <em>stare decisis</em> in abortion cases.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To be clear, public opposition to a prior decision is not a basis for overruling (or reaffirming) that decision.  Rather, the question of whether to overrule a precedent must be analyzed under this Court's traditional <em>stare decisis</em> factors.  The only point here is that <em>Casey</em> adopted a special <em>stare decisis</em> principle with respect to <em>Roe</em> based on the idea of resolving the national controversy and ending the national division over abortion.  The continued and significant opposition to <em>Roe</em>, as reflected in the laws and positions of numerous States, is relevant to assessing <em>Casey</em> on its own terms.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>The majority contends that "nothing like [my approach] was recommended by either party."  <em>Ante, </em>at 72.  But as explained, Mississippi in fact pressed a similar argument in its filings before this Court.  See Pet. for Cert. 15-26; Brief for Petitioners 5, 38-48 (urging the Court to reject the viability rule and reverse); Reply Brief 20-22 (same).  The approach also finds support in prior opinions.  See <em>Webster</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S., at 518-521 (plurality opinion) (abandoning "key elements" of the <em>Roe </em>framework under <em>stare decisis</em> while declining to reconsider <em>Roe</em>'s holding that the Constitution protects the right to an abortion).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For this reason, we do not understand the majority's view that our analogy between the right to an abortion and the rights to contraception and same-sex marriage shows that we think "[t]he Constitution does not permit the States to regard the destruction of a 'potential life' as a matter of any significance."  <em>Ante</em>, at 38.  To the contrary.  The liberty interests underlying those rights are, as we will describe, quite similar.  See <em>infra</em>, at 22-24.  But only in the sphere of abortion is the state interest in protecting potential life involved.  So only in that sphere, as both <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey </em>recognized, may a State impinge so far on the liberty interest (barring abortion after viability and discouraging it before).  The majority's failure to understand this fairly obvious point stems from its rejection of the idea of balancing interests in this (or maybe in any) constitutional context.  Cf. <em>New York State Rifle &amp; Pistol Assn., Inc. </em>v. <em>Bruen</em>, 597 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___, ___-___ (2022) (slip op., at 8, 15-17).<em>  </em>The majority thinks that a woman has <em>no</em> liberty or equality interest in the decision to bear a child, so a State's interest in protecting fetal life necessarily prevails.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See,<em> e.g.</em>, 1 W. Blackstone, Commentaries on the Laws of England 129-130 (7th ed. 1775) (Blackstone); E. Coke, Institutes of the Laws of England 50 (1644).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See J. Mohr, Abortion in America: The Origins and Evolution of National Policy, 1800-1900, pp. 3-4 (1978).  The majority offers no evidence to the contrary--no example of a founding-era law making prequickening abortion a crime (except when a woman died).  See <em>ante</em>, at 20-21.  And even in the mid-19th century, more than 10 States continued to allow pre-quickening abortions.  See Brief for American Historical Association et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 27, and n.&nbsp;14.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority ignores that rejection.  See <em>ante</em>, at 5, 13, 36.  But it is unequivocal: The <em>Glucksberg </em>test, <em>Obergefell </em>said, "may have been appropriate" in considering physician-assisted suicide, but "is inconsistent with the approach this Court has used in discussing other fundamental rights, including marriage and intimacy."  576 U.&nbsp;S., at 671.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In a perplexing paragraph in its opinion, the majority declares that it need not say whether that statement from <em>Casey</em> is true.  See <em>ante</em>, at 32-33.  But how could that be?  Has not the majority insisted for the prior 30 or so pages that the "specific practice[&nbsp;]" respecting abortion at the time of the Fourteenth Amendment precludes its recognition as a constitutional right?  <em>Ante</em>, at 33.<em>  </em>It has.  And indeed, it has given no other reason for overruling <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  <em>Ante</em>, at 15-16.  We are not mindreaders, but here is our best guess as to what the majority means.  It says next that "[a]bortion is nothing new."  <em>Ante</em>, at 33.  So apparently, the Fourteenth Amendment might provide protection for things wholly unknown in the 19th century; maybe one day there could be constitutional protection for, oh, time travel.  But as to anything that was known back then (such as abortion or contraception), no such luck.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;And note, too, that the author of the majority opinion recently joined a statement, written by another member of the majority, lamenting that <em>Obergefell </em>deprived States of the ability "to resolve th[e] question [of same-sex marriage] through legislation."  <em>Davis</em> v. <em>Ermold</em>, 592 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2020) (statement of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J.) (slip op., at 1).  That might sound familiar.  Cf. <em>ante</em>, at 44 (lamenting that <em>Roe</em> "short-circuited the democratic process").  And those two Justices hardly seemed content to let the matter rest: The Court, they said, had "created a problem that only it can fix."  <em>Davis</em>, 592 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 4).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Indulge a few more words about this point.  The majority had a choice of two different ways to overrule <em>Roe </em>and <em>Casey</em>.  It could claim that those cases<em> </em>underrated the State's interest in fetal life.  Or it could claim that they overrated a woman's constitutional liberty interest in choosing an abortion.  (Or both.)  The majority here rejects the first path, and we can see why.  Taking that route would have prevented the majority from claiming that it means only to leave this issue to the democratic process--that it does not have a dog in the fight.  See <em>ante</em>, at 38-39, 65.  And indeed, doing so might have suggested a revolutionary proposition: that the fetus is itself a constitutionally protected "person," such that an abortion ban is constitutionally <em>mandated.  </em>The majority therefore chooses the second path, arguing that the Fourteenth Amendment does not conceive of the abortion decision as implicating liberty, because the law in the 19th century gave that choice no protection.  The trouble is that the chosen path--which is, again, the solitary rationale for the Court's decision--provides no way to distinguish between the right to choose an abortion and a range of other rights, including contraception.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority briefly (very briefly) gestures at the idea that some <em>stare decisis</em> factors might play out differently with respect to these other constitutional rights.  But the majority gives no hint as to why.  And the majority's (mis)treatment of <em>stare decisis</em> in this case provides little reason to think that the doctrine would stand as a barrier to the majority's redoing any other decision it considered egregiously wrong.  See <em>infra</em>, at 30-57.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As this Court has considered this case, some state legislators have begun to call for restrictions on certain forms of contraception.  See  I. Stevenson, After <em>Roe </em>Decision, Idaho Lawmakers May Consider  Restricting Some Contraception, Idaho Statesman (May 10, 2022), https://www.idahostatesman.com/news/politics-government/state-politics/ article261207007.html; T. Weinberg, "Anything's on the Table": Missouri Legislature May Revisit Contraceptive Limits Post-<em>Roe</em>, Missouri Independent (May 20, 2022), https://www.missouriindependent.com/2022/05/ 20/anythings-on-the-table-missouri-legislature-may-revisit-contraceptivelimits-post-roe/.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Some lower courts then differed over which opinion in <em>June Medical</em> was controlling--but that is a dispute not about the undue burden standard, but about the "<em>Marks</em> rule," which tells courts how to determine the precedential effects of a divided decision.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The rest of the majority's supposed splits are, shall we say, unimpressive.  The majority says that lower courts have split over how to apply the undue burden standard to parental notification laws.  See <em>ante</em>, at 60, and n. 54.  But that is not so.  The state law upheld had an exemption for minors demonstrating adequate maturity, whereas the ones struck down did not.  Compare <em>Planned Parenthood of Blue Ridge </em>v. <em>Camblos</em>, 155 F.&nbsp;3d 352, 383-384 (CA4 1998), with <em>Planned Parenthood of Ind. &amp; Ky., Inc.</em> v. <em>Adams</em>, 937 F.&nbsp;3d 973, 981 (CA7 2019), cert. granted, judgment vacated, 591 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2020), and <em>Planned Parenthood, Sioux Falls Clinic </em>v. <em>Miller</em>, 63 F.&nbsp;3d 1452, 1460 (CA8 1995).  The majority says there is a split about bans on certain types of abortion procedures.  See <em>ante</em>, at 61, and n. 55.  But the one court to have separated itself on that issue did so based on a set of factual findings significantly different from those in other cases.  Compare <em>Whole Woman's Health </em>v. <em>Paxton</em>, 10 F.&nbsp;4th 430, 447-453 (CA5 2021), with <em>EMW Women's Surgical Center</em>,<em> P.S.C.</em> v. <em>Friedlander</em>, 960 F.&nbsp;3d 785, 798-806 (CA6 2020), and <em>West Ala. Women's Center </em>v. <em>Williamson</em>, 900 F.&nbsp;3d 1310, 1322-1324 (CA11 2018).  Finally, the majority says there is a split about whether an increase in travel time to reach a clinic is an undue burden.  See <em>ante</em>, at 61, and n.&nbsp;56.  But the cases to which the majority refers predate this Court's decision in <em>Whole Woman's Health</em> v. <em>Hellerstedt</em>, 579 U.&nbsp;S. 582 (2016), which clarified how to apply the undue burden standard to that context.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To take just the last, most medical treatments for miscarriage are identical to those used in abortions.  See Kaiser Family Foundation (Kaiser), G. Weigel, L. Sobel, &amp; A. Salganicoff, Understanding Pregnancy Loss in the Context of Abortion Restrictions and Fetal Harm Laws  (Dec. 4, 2019), https://www.kff.org/womens-health-policy/issue-brief/ understanding-pregnancy-loss-in-the-context-of-abortion-restrictions-andfetal-harm-laws/.  Blanket restrictions on "abortion" procedures and medications therefore may be understood to deprive women of effective treatment for miscarriages, which occur in about 10 to 30 percent of pregnancies.  See Health Affairs, J. Strasser, C. Chen, S. Rosenbaum, E. Schenk, &amp; E. Dewhurst, Penalizing Abortion Providers Will Have Ripple Effects Across Pregnancy Care (May 3, 2022), https://www.healthaffairs. org/do/10.1377/forefront.20220503.129912/.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See L. Harris, Navigating Loss of Abortion Services--A Large Academic Medical Center Prepares for the Overturn of <em>Roe </em>v. <em>Wade</em>, 386 New England J. Med. 2061, 2063 (2022).  This projected racial disparity reflects existing differences in maternal mortality rates for black and white women.  Black women are now three to four times more likely to die during or after childbirth than white women, often from preventable causes.  See Brief for Howard University School of Law Human and Civil Rights Clinic as <em>Amicus Curiae </em>18.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services, Issue Brief: Improving Access to Maternal Health Care in Rural Communities 4, 8, 11 (Sept. 2019), https://www.cms.gov/About-CMS/Agency-Information/ OMH/equity-initiatives/rural-health/09032019-Maternal-Health-Care-in-Rural-Communities.pdf.  In Mississippi, for instance, 19 percent of women of reproductive age are uninsured and 60 percent of counties lack a single obstetrician-gynecologist.  Brief for Lawyers' Committee for Civil Rights Under Law et al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 12-13.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Dept. of Labor, National Compensation Survey: Employee Benefits in the United States, Table 31 (Sept. 2020), https://www.bls.gov/ncs/ebs/ benefits/2020/employee-benefits-in-the-united-states-march-2020.pdf# page=299.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Safe haven laws, which allow parents to leave newborn babies in designated safe spaces without threat of prosecution, were not enacted as an alternative to abortion, but in response to rare situations in which birthing mothers in crisis would kill their newborns or leave them to die.  See Centers for Disease Control and Prevention (CDC), R. Wilson, J. Klevens, D. Williams, &amp; L. Xu, Infant Homicides Within the Context of Safe Haven Laws--United States, 2008-2017, 69 Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report 1385 (2020).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNopinion1.17" href="#FRopinion1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A study of women who sought an abortion but were denied one because of gestational limits found that only 9 percent put the child up for adoption, rather than parenting themselves.  See G. Sisson, L. Ralph, H. Gould, &amp; D. Foster, Adoption Decision Making Among Women Seeking Abortion, 27 Women's Health Issues 136, 139 (2017).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNopinion1.18" href="#FRopinion1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority finally notes the claim that "people now have a new appreciation of fetal life," partly because of viewing sonogram images.  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 34.  It is hard to know how anyone would evaluate such a claim and as we have described above, the majority's reasoning does not rely on any reevaluation of the interest in protecting fetal life.  See <em>supra</em>, at 26, and n.&nbsp;7.  It is worth noting that sonograms became widely used in the 1970s, long before <em>Casey</em>.  Today, 60 percent of women seeking abortions have at least one child, and one-third have two or more.  See CDC, K. Kortsmit et&nbsp;al., Abortion Surveillance--United States, 2019, 70 Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report 6 (2021).  These women know, even as they choose to have an abortion, what it is to look at a sonogram image and to value a fetal life.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNopinion1.19" href="#FRopinion1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Guttmacher Institute, K. Kost, Unintended Pregnancy Rates at the State Level: Estimates for 2010 and Trends Since 2002, Table 1 (2015), https://www.guttmacher.org/sites/default/files/report_pdf/stateup10.pdf; Kaiser, State Requirements for Insurance Coverage of Contraceptives (May 1, 2022), https://www.kff.org/state-category/womens-health/family-planning; Miss. Code Ann. §37-13-171(2)(d) (Cum. Supp. 2021) ("In no case shall the instruction or program include any demonstration of how condoms or other contraceptives are applied").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 20" name="FNopinion1.20" href="#FRopinion1.20">20</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See CDC, Infant Mortality Rates by State (Mar. 3, 2022), https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/pressroom/sosmap/infant_mortality_rates/infant _mortality.htm; Mississippi State Dept. of Health, Infant Mortality Report 2019 &amp; 2020, pp. 18-19 (2021), https://www.msdh.ms.gov/ msdhsite/_static/resources/18752.pdf; CDC, Percentage of Babies Born Low Birthweight by State (Feb. 25, 2022), https://www.cdc.gov/ nchs/pressroom/sosmap/lbw_births/lbw.htm; CDC, Cesarean Delivery Rate by State (Feb. 25, 2022), https://www.cdc.gov/nchs/pressroom/ sosmap/cesarean_births/cesareans.htm; Mississippi State Dept. of Health, Mississippi Maternal Mortality Report 2013-2016, pp. 5, 25 (Mar. 2021), https://www.msdh.ms.gov/msdhsite/_static/resources/8127.pdf.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNopinion1.21" href="#FRopinion1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See D. Grossman, K. Grindlay, &amp; B. Burns, Public Funding for Abortion Where Broadly Legal, 94 Contraception 451, 458 (2016) (discussing funding of abortion in European countries).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 22" name="FNopinion1.22" href="#FRopinion1.22">22</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See CDC, K. Kortsmit et&nbsp;al., Abortion Surveillance--United States, 2019, 70 Morbidity and Mortality Weekly Report 7 (2021); Brief for American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists et al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 9.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 23" name="FNopinion1.23" href="#FRopinion1.23">23</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Astoundingly, the majority casts this statement as a "conce[ssion]" from <em>Casey </em>with which it "agree[s]."  <em>Ante</em>,<em> </em>at 64.  In fact, <em>Casey </em>used this language as part of describing an argument that it <em>rejected</em>.  See 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 856.  It is only today's Court that endorses this profoundly mistaken view.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 24" name="FNopinion1.24" href="#FRopinion1.24">24</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Brief for 547 Deans 6-7 (noting that 51 percent of women who terminated their pregnancies reported using contraceptives during the month in which they conceived); Brief for Lawyers' Committee for Civil Rights Under Law et al. as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 12-14 (explaining financial and geographic barriers to access to effective contraceptives).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This statement of course assumes that States are not successful in preventing interstate travel to obtain an abortion.  See <em>supra</em>, at 3, 36-37.  Even assuming that is so, increased out-of-state demand will lead to longer wait times and decreased availability of service in States still providing abortions.  See Brief for State of California et&nbsp;al. as <em>Amici Curiae </em>25-27.  This is what happened in Oklahoma, Kansas, Colorado, New Mexico, and Nevada last fall after Texas effectively banned abortions past six weeks of gestation.  See <em>United States </em>v. <em>Texas</em>, 595 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2021) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., concurring in part and dissenting in part) (slip op., at 6).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The average cost of a first-trimester abortion is about $500.  See Brief for Abortion Funds 7.  Federal insurance generally does not cover the cost of abortion, and 35 percent of American adults do not have cash on hand to cover an unexpected expense that high.  Guttmacher Institute, M. Donovan, In Real Life: Federal Restrictions on Abortion Coverage and the Women They Impact (Jan. 5, 2017), https://www. guttmacher.org/gpr/2017/01/real-life-federal-restrictions-abortion-coverage-and-women-they-impact#:~:text=Although%20the%20Hyde%20Amendment% 20bars,provide%20abortion%20coverage%20to%20enrollees; Brief for Abortion Funds 11.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Mississippi is likely to be one of the States where these costs are highest, though history shows that it will have company.  As described above, Mississippi provides only the barest financial support to pregnant women.  See <em>supra</em>, at 41-42.  The State will greatly restrict abortion care without addressing any of the financial, health, and family needs that motivate many women to seek it.  The effects will be felt most severely, as they always have been, on the bodies of the poor.  The history of state abortion restrictions is a history of heavy costs exacted from the most vulnerable women.  It is a history of women seeking illegal abortions in hotel rooms and home kitchens; of women trying to self-induce abortions by douching with bleach, injecting lye, and penetrating themselves with knitting needles, scissors, and coat hangers.  See L. Reagan, When Abortion Was a Crime 42-43, 198-199, 208-209 (1997).  It is a history of women dying.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNopinion1.28" href="#FRopinion1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority's sole citation for its "concreteness" requirement is <em>Payne </em>v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808 (1991).  But <em>Payne </em>merely discounted reliance interests in cases involving "procedural and evidentiary rules."  <em>Id.</em>, at 828.  Unlike the individual right at stake here, those rules do "not alter primary conduct."  <em>Hohn</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 524 U.&nbsp;S. 236, 252 (1998).  Accordingly, they generally "do not implicate the reliance interests of private parties" at all.  <em>Alleyne</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 570 U.&nbsp;S. 99, 119 (2013) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J., </small></span>concurring).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNopinion1.29" href="#FRopinion1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Guttmacher Institute, E. Nash, State Policy Trends 2021: The Worst Year for Abortion Rights in Almost Half a Century (Dec. 16, 2021), https://www.guttmacher.org/article/2021/12/state-policy-trends-2021-worst-year-abortion-rights-almost-half-century; Guttmacher Institute, E. Nash, L. Mohammed, O. Cappello, &amp; S. Naide, State Policy Trends 2020: Reproductive Health and Rights in a Year Like No Other (Dec. 15, 2020), https://www.guttmacher.org/article/2020/12/state-policy-trends-2020reproductive-health-and-rights-year-no-other; Guttmacher Institute, E. Nash, L. Mohammed, O. Cappello, &amp; S. Naide, State Policy Trends 2019: A Wave of Abortion Bans, But Some States Are Fighting Back (Dec. 10, 2019), https://www.guttmacher.org/article/2019/12/state-policy-trends-2019-wave-abortion-bans-some-states-are-fighting-back.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNopinion1.30" href="#FRopinion1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A. Pittman, Mississippi's Six-Week Abortion Ban at 5th Circuit Appeals Court Today, Jackson Free Press (Oct. 7, 2019), https://www. jacksonfreepress.com/news/2019/oct/07/mississippis-six-week-abortion-ban-5th-circuit-app/.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNopinion1.31" href="#FRopinion1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We have since come to understand <em>Gideon </em>as part of a larger doctrinal shift--already underway at the time of <em>Gideon</em>--where "the Court began to hold that the Due Process Clause fully incorporates particular rights contained in the first eight Amendments."  <em>McDonald</em> v. <em>Chicago</em>, 561 U.&nbsp;S. 742, 763 (2010); see also <em>id.</em>, at 766.</p>

</main><script>_satellite["_runScript1"](function(event, target, Promise) {
_satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';
  var c = _satellite.cookie.get(cookieName);
  // we use a cookie to ensure multiple beacons are not sent for the same ad
  if( typeof c == 'undefined' ) {
    _satellite.setFlCookie(cookieName, cookieValue, 1);

    var data = {};
    var clickUrlElement = document.getElementById(_satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub'));
    if (clickUrlElement !== null) {
      data.clickUrl = clickUrlElement.innerText;
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') ) {
      data.adDecisionContext = _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id') ) {
      data.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub') ) {
      data.subId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id') ) {
      data.flUserId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_tag_track') == 'false' ) {
      data.internalFlag = 'true'; // mimick as an internal request
    }

    let url = new URL(window.location.href);
    let domain = 'attorneys.findlaw'+(_satellite.getVar('cms_env') == 'prod' ? '' : '-stage')+'.com';
    let attorneysUrl = new URL('https://'+domain+'/flstats/v1/stats');
    let searchParams = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);

    if (searchParams.has('ads-server-env')) {
      attorneysUrl.searchParams.set('ads-server-env', url.searchParams.get('ads-server-env'));
    }
    navigator.sendBeacon(attorneysUrl.href, JSON.stringify(data));
  }
}

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);
    linksWithSearchLocation.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = e.currentTarget.dataset.searchLocation || e.currentTarget.dataset.closest("[data-search-location]").dataset.searchLocation;
        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
